TREATISE ON NERVOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE,
SYMPTOMS, TREATMENT. AND CURE.
TUIE directions contained in this little treatise will be
. found of immense benefit to the sufferer; and if these
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervous
or Sexual Debility, No Medicines vrill he required, as, by
observing the directions given, the patient can Cure Him-
self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-
cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and long
and successful experience in the treatment of diseases,
and contains a fund of useful information to every suf-
ferer from the following complaints, viz.:—Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Discontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.
If the complaint is of long standing and requires
medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURE
ANY CASE OF NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITY
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is unlike anv
other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investigate his statements by verifying
the testimonials published in his treatise. The treatise
will be forwarded on receipt of two stamps To cover
postage.

Law House Suroeon to the Great Northern Hospital, Caledonian Road, London,
SPECIALIST in the. Treatment of Diseases of the
Reproductive Organs and Nervous Complaints.
Du. PERKY was induced to publicly advertise himself as a
Specialist in this branch of the profession in consequence of the number
of persons suffering from diseases of this nature remaining nncured : and
this is not surprising, when it is remembered, in consequence of the
general dislike of the profession to undertake these cases, the public
health has suffered, localise the majority of Persons advertising to treat
these diseases, and calling themselves Doctors, Professors, etc.,
Are Neither the One nor the Other,
and exist chiefly because DI'LV QFALIFI KD MEDICAL PRAC-
TITIONERS have not occupied the field.
Patients can, therefore, readily see the advantage of being treated
bv a practitioner who has gone through a thorough Medical, Surgical and
\ natomieal Training in the best hospitals : and the Doctor argues that,
although it may be considered contrary to antiquated medical etiquette
to so advertise, yet lie is promoting the usefulness AND THK iuonitv of
the profession more by searching out and relieving this class of disease
Ilian by leaving them severely alone to the care of men who, however good
their intentions, have neither the opportunities or advantages of a regular
medical training to become acquainted with the best methods of treat ing
these complex forms of disease.

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in tne
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we aronut responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
sterling— which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 9, 1890.
Party Government.
Ifc is one of the proudest boasts of the average
Briton that the constitution of his beloved
country was not built on any detinite plan,
but that it “ grew up ” in a gradual, uncer-
tain sort of way according to the require-
ments of the nation. It grew somewhat lop-
sided, too, and became shapeless and patchy
in its formation, but the Briton regards it
with a feeling of blissful contentment because
of its growth. That lamented person, the
recent Prince Consort, after a vain effort to
discover how his adopted country was
governed or who governed it, once offered
the remark that “ representative institutions
were-upon their trial,’ and whether he made
this observation all by himself or whether lie
hired somebody to assist him, it was a really
brilliant suggestion for a prince to make.
And it deserves to be kept in remembrance,
for it was the only original expression he is
ever known to have made use of, and when
he died at his post a little while later, with
income in his right hand, he was still
trying to fathom the chaos of party politics,
md still wondering why he had never seen
inything resembling the British Constitution
before.
* # *
The first great principle of government in
all countries which have adopted the British
system is that there must be always some-
body on hand ready to rise and contradict
the Cabinet, and that the Cabinet and its
supporters must bo prepared at any hour of
the night or day to state in general terms
that the other party is a liar. This process
of incessant contradiction is understood to
be essential to purity of administration, and
when there is a falling-off in the violence of
the contradiction it is felt, that representative
institutions are on the decline. in other
words, it is universally recognised that the
Government is of a low character, and that
unless somebody is constantly present to ex-
pose its villainy the country will generally go
to wreck. And after due consideration of
government in general, there seems to be
sufficient reason for this assumption in almost
every case. Moreover, when the Govern-
ment is ejected from office and the other
party takes its place, the same precautions
are invariably regarded as essential, from
which it would appear that the other party
is likewise a despicable object, and not to be
trusted. A comprehensive glance at the
history of the other party in general affords
good reason for a belief in the soundness of
this theor' likewise : and consequently
party politics may be briefly defined as a
system by which two low-minded organisa-
tions are hired to expound each other’s
infamy for the public benefit, and to prove
to the country that the other side is an asso-
ciation of grasping brigands, to whom honour
.is a myth, and ordinary decency a delusion,
and truth an old forgotten tiling of no ac-
count. And as a rule both sides prove this
fact with such vigour and success that it
becomes a matter of surprise that the nation
does not immediately discharge both of them
and start afresh on some basis which will
oiler less scope for an exhibition of all the
bad qualities of humanity.
* * *
It is one of the gilded fictions of this cum-
brous system that each faction is contending
for a principle, but even admitting this
obvious lie to be true it goes rather to prove
the. awful scarcity of principle than to
demonstrate its actual existence. For in the
majority of representative assemblies two
factions and a few political outcasts make up
the entire total, and even granting that each
body has a principle of some kind to contend
for,, t wo of these articles divided among, say,
130 members is a woefully short allowance.
Yet, considering the unanimity with which
the party in office hangs on to its salary, and
the equal- unanimity with which tire party
out of office demands that salary, it is
reasonable to assume that each side has only
one fixed principle amongst it, or else that it
has sunk all the rest of its convictions lest
they should result in disorganisation, and dis-
organisation should lead to general over-
throw. Or again, it may be that neither, of
the opposing leagues has any conviction at
all except that it wants £l5OO a year and
any other pickings that may come in its way,
and in most cases, no doubt, this is the more
correct explanation of the two. A politician
with convictions of any kind is necessarily a
failure, for if his opponents only remain long
enough in office they are almost certain,
sooner or later, to borrow his principle from
him, and then it is his duty as a party poli-
tician to curse them for doing what he pre-
viously cursed them for leaving undone, and
to prove their villainy in accomplishing what
he formerly proved them to be inhuman
ruffians for not accomplishing, and if his
moral sentiments stand in the way it is time
for him to retire and make room for some
person of greater scone and fluency.
* * *
The reason why few Governments survive
a general election is that the country is
weary of both sides alike, and w r ould gladly
consign the whole race of party politicians
to oblivion if it only knew how to do it, or
how to get anything else instead. But -the
system of Government by Contradiction has
become so deeply-rooted that the nation
finds it difficult to conceive the possibility of
any other form of administration. It is the
recognised axiom in all Anglo-Saxon com-
munities that the Legislature must neces-
sarily consist of two factions, and that each
should assert that everything the other does
is wrong ; and the fact that each faction
generally proves the truth of its assertion is
only a trivial aggravation of the evil. The
choice does not even lie between the devil
the country knows and the devil it does not
know ; as a rule it is intimately acquainted
with both devils,juyid hates the pair of them.
But as the mental and moral wretchedness
of the party in office is the more conspicuous
of the two, the country gradually gets up a
species of melancholy enthusiasm on behalf
of the other side.' It would gladly heave a
brick at both of them, but being driven to a
choice it votes for the,person who seems to
deserve the smaller brick. The whole
system of party requires that every man
should sink his principles—if he ever had
any- and his honour, supposing him to be
furnished with such an article, for the sake
of “dishing” the man opposite, and a poli-
tician being thus necessarily a man without
any honour or principle worth mentioning, a
constituency must either choose between
two unprincipled individuals, or return
somebody who will belong to neither party,
and who will consequently be neglected by
botli when the loaves and fishes are going
round.
w # *
A few other disadvantages of more or less
importance attach themselves to this un-
wieldy system of administration. The leader
who has scrambled into power, either be-
cause he is the less of two grievous evils, or
because the other evil looks larger for the
moment through having been placed too
conspicuously before tiro naked eye, has the
sole power of selecting his colleagues, and he
may fill the highest offices of the State with
publicans, usurers, and drunkards at his
pleasure. The party has either to accept or
reject this gang in their entirety, and as
rejection means handing over the reins of
authority to the hated individuals opposite,
they generally take the lot. If the House
wishes to rid itself by force of the most con-
spicuous imbecile in the Ministry it can only
do it by Rejecting the whole Cabinet, and
giving the leader on the other side an oppor-
tunity to form a Ministry of his own, with
some equally irremoveable human incubus
in it. Consequently the final result is a
strangely, anomalous one. The Parliament
itself° is chosen by the people, generally
because they don t know any better. Ihe
Premier is selected within the House, not by
a direct vote, but generally by accident, or
because his voice is so loud and vociferous
that it drowns all objection. And finally the
Premier’s colleagues are appointed without
any a ppeal either to the House or the people,
sometimes, it may be, because they have had
financial dealings with their chief, sometimes
because their muddle-headedness is a guar-
antee that they will not interfere with un-
called-for suggestions, and sometimes for no
earthly reason that either they or anyone
else can ever discover. The spectacle of Sir
p,i;van <VLoohlrn as Premier of Victoria
without a following, and of Six’ Patrick Jen- .
kings as the newly-elevated leader of an
Assembly that had hardly recognised his
existence, are fair samples of the possibi-
lities of the modern Parliamentary system.
# * *
The peaceful little Republic of Switzerland
is probably the only State in existence which
has learned how to run a constitutional
government utterly irrespective of party
considerations. In that plain, undignified
commonwealth the Premier is elected by a
direct vote of the Assembly, and thus those
strange eventualities, by which a tenth-rate
politician may rise to sudden eminence
because he moved a vote of censure which
met with unexpected success, and fall again
as suddenly when he has reduced the affairs
of the nation to chaos, have no place in Swiss
politics. His colleagues are elected in a
similar manner, and, consequently, the
Premier is a man without any personal
following, for the average politician only
follows someone who has offices to distribute.
The Cabinet holds office for three years, and
it is never defeated in the ordinary accepta-
tion of the term, because the House has
never pledged itself to follow its dictates.
Its chief duty is to transact all necessary
routine business and to attend to the details
of administration, but if it introduces measures
which are contrary to the general sense of the
Diet, they are never passed on any mere party
considerations, for the sufficient reason that no
parties exist. Its measures are thrown out
with the same promptitude and impartiality
as those of the most obscure private member
in an Australian legislature, when they fail
to create a favourable impression on the
majority of the Swiss lawmakers, and still
the Cabinet pursues the even tenor of its
way until its term of office expires. The
insane idea that a successful Government
should necessarily resign because it has been
defeated on, some obscure clog-tax proposal,
or other equally unimportant matter, is
essentially a party axiom, and has never even
been dreamt of in the Swiss Constitution,
and as the Republican Assembly recognises
the impossibility of ever forming an imma-
culate Cabinet, it declines to pledge itself to
follow any Cabinet through good or evil
fortune till it lands itself in ruin. The ill-
success of the British principle, according to
which the Parliament must either follow the
Government blindly for better or worse, or
else construct a new Government which it is
prepared to obey in this same unquestioning
fashion, suggests that the Helvetian Diet
has adopted the only possible solution of the
difficulty. No Ministry that ever was or
ever will be constructed deserves that ser-
vile obedience which is the only condition
under which an Australian or British
Government can possibly maintain its
position,
a # *
Moreover’, the Swiss Constitution recog-
nises the absolute impossibility of finding
eight or nine statesmen in one small Assem-
bly who can profess, without unscrupulous
mendacity, to be practically unanimous on
all questions of public policy, and it makes
no effort to secure such a collection of
phenomena. If two separate members of
the Cabinet insist on proposing measures of
a diametrically opposite character they can
do so, and the House can reject one or both
at its pleasure. Should any one or any half-
dozen Ministers desire to retire there is no
unseemly scramble for a dissolution, for
nobody has anything to gain by dissolving ;
the vacant places are tilled up by a vote of
the House, and the little commonwealth goes
on its way as before. There are no frantic
efforts to snatch a party victory, for the sys-
tem allows no scope for the formation of
parties, and even if they did exist no
one has any special use for a party at
all, and a vote of confidence is of no
value in an Assembly which shows its
entire confidence in each individual Minister
by the appointment of the Cabinet, and
which can get on about equally well whether
it has confidence or not. There is no oppor-
tunity for intrigue, and no necessity for buy-
ing up venal opponents or unreliable adher-
ents, for the Government’s position is secure
till the end of its term, and yet it has little
chance of abusing its trust, for it has no
personal supporters whatever, and the House
has no crisis to fear when it throws one Go-
vernment measure after another into the
ash-heap. And as a result of all these things
there are no violent assaults, no strong
language, and no excitement whatever,
neither is there the slightest necessity for
executing expensive public works on top of
an inaccessible mountain peak to gratify any
Influential member, for that disappointed
individual can’t go over to the Opposi-
tion by way of a picturesque revenge—’
Switzerland lias never owned an Opposition
since the present constitution came into exis-
tence a century ago. And after viewing the
matter in all its bearings, it appears to us
that this country might reasonably abandon
that political system of shreds and patches
which she copied from the grand old
mother land, and let government by contra-
diction have a rest, and attempt something
on the Swiss model in its stead, as the basis
of the Constitution of Federated Australia.
If there is any latent capacity for unselfish-
ness and honesty in the political world, it
would then have at least a chance to mani-
fest itself ; and if any ordinary measure of
honesty is too much to expect, there worth!
at least be some consolation in the knowledge
that our statesmen were dishonest under
c» msiderable difficulties.
Ex-Judge Trench, on “The Revolt of the
Jury.”
We have received the following letter from
the veteran ex-Judge Trench, Q.C., of Vic-
toria <■
Editor Bulletin; Sir, —ln your issue
of 19th ult. you published a leading article
entitled “The Revolt of the- Jury.” I
now write to express my high appreciation of
its merits as an able exposure of a most humi-
liating complication in the administration of
justice in our criminal courts. I concur fully in
all you have urged with such irresistible force
in that article; and I may remark that my
views on the subject have been formed in the
course of many years’ experience at the Bar and
on the Bench, and, moreover, have been well
matured by persistent study and reflection since
my retirement.
* * #
If you will do me the honour to glance over
the small pamphlet which I send herewith you
will see that I have made an effort to show how
the embarrassing complication, which I rejoice to
know has come under correction of The Bul-
letin, might, and ought to be removed.
# s *
I style my unpretentious pamphlet “ A
Standard of measurements of Sentences in
Criminal cases and a Reformed System of Penal
Discipline,” and I endeavour to show that the
root of the evil which you have, with such pre-
cision exposed, is hidden in the misplaced dis-
cretion with which the judges are saddled by
the peculiar phraseology of the statutes in limit-
ing the duration of imprisonment as a punish-
ment for particular crimes. The term of im-
prisonment. is expressed thus ; “ not exceedin'/
years,” and hence the judges are charged
with the awful responsibility of determining
whether a sentence shall be for the full term so
limited ; or, if not, by how much less than that
period a convict shall be imprisoned and sub-
jected to the horrors of penal discipline in some
one of the gaols. The science of correction forms
no part of a strictly legal education, yet the
judges, all untrained, are saddled with a discre-
tion covering the whole period of life—from t iro
sentence ‘ ‘ till the rising of the court, to ‘ ‘ for life,”
which must be exercised unaided by any fixed
principle or common rule to guide them. Under
such circumstances, what might be deemed the
exercise of a, sound discretion is impossible ; and
the result is that the administration of our
criminal law is a pronounced, most disastrous,
failure. Punishment lias not proved deterrent,
nor yet reformatory : but, on the contrary, the
“habitual criminal class” lias increased to an
alarming extent. Mr. Justice Webr, passing
sentence in “The Uembrook Tragedy ” case, is
reported in the Age of 24th inst. to have ad-
dressed the prisoner thus; “AN hilst sentencing
him he would keep him from contamination and
not start him on the downward career of crime,
which lie feared imprisonment in our gaols now
very frequently did/’ Add to this the cold-
blooded narrative of “The Suicide of Pierre
Douar,” reported in the Age of the 19th inst.
In the former case the culprit had the good
fortune to Ire in the hands of a judge gifted among
men with the instinct of the humane ; but the
wretched man Douar was systematically driven
to self-murder as the only mode of escape from
life “in the A division (which is worked on the
silent system”) under a sentence of two years in
irons, awarded by two visiting jvsticcs !
# * *
The law, as a whole, having to do with rights
and wrongs in civil and criminal cases —wrongs
becpmingcrimeswhcn they affect the public peace
injuriously —it is boasted of the civil jurisdic-
tion that for every infringement of a right there
is a remedy by action to recover redress in
pecuniary damages assessed by a jury. But in
cases where the personal wrong amounts to a
crime the civil remedy becomes valueless, being,
as a high authority has expressed it, “swal-
lowed up ”by the public prosecution. There is
no sufficient justification for this practice, as, I
think, is made manifest in my pamphlet. By
the expedient of two issues—a civil one for
damages and a criminal one as to the guilt of
the accused—being submitted to the jury at one
trial, punishment would practically be measured
by the jury. The evidence would be the same
on the trial of both issues. If the jury awarded,
say, exemplary damages, the verdict would be
entered in the usual way, and the verdict being
guilty on the criminal issue, the judge would
have nothing to do but to pass sentence of im-
prisonment with hard labour for the full term
limited by the statute, adding the qualification
following, “unless by appropriations of money
earned by the prisoner in gaol, to the liquida-
tion of the judgment debt, satisfaction shall be
sooner entered on the civil record,” or words to
the like effect. By this mode the duration
of punishment would be made to depend upon
the amount of the damages under the verdict of
the jury on the civil issue ; the judges would be
extricated from a false position : the private
prosecutor who had been assaulted and robbed
or •otherwise injured would have his damages
assessed by a jury with, probably, as good a
prospect of realising the amount as the average
litigant may count on ; and, moreover, under a
“Reformed System of Penal Discipline” the
unfortunate culprit is taken hold of by the
State, and compelled to labour with his hands
to make full amends. His imprisonment is for
safe custody ; the hard Labour part of the sen-
tence would be work honestly paid for at rates
of wages ruling in the open market, on the
principle of a fair day’s wages for a fair day’s
work, thus letting in hope and encouragement
and the consolation of knowing that the harder
he worked the sooner he would shake himself
free of his bonds. (laola would become as they
should Ire, self-supporting, and the whole cost
of crime be taken out of the criminals —Yours,
&c., Robert LePoer Trench.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Alleged Grand Duke.
Tub Grand Duke Nicholas At.kxaxhrovitch,
heir to tlie Russian throne, dullest and
most unpromising of Romanoffs, is alleged
to be contemplating a visit to Australia.
Should he come it is a pleasant and cheer-


iyg rejection that Australia will, no doubt,
have the grovel ready. To all the Anglo-
Saxon race the Romanoff who is afar off, is an
accursed emblem of the darkest tyranny on
earth, and his very name is a synonym for the
knout and the dungeon ; but to the Romanoff
w ho is near at hand, the nation wallows all the
same. The story of the exiles in the mines of
Siberia and the foetid marshes of Saghalien, the
tale of the “ hunger-strikes ” in the pestilential
prisons of Omsk and Tobolsk, the pitiable
narrative of ladies flogged and tortured to death
in the gaols of Northern Asia, may be real
enough at other times, but if the heir of a line
of butchers should choose to visit this country,
the “respectable” press will hail him with rever-
ence and admiration, women will take his blood-
stained arm at Government House, and the
great army of municipal flunkeyism will bow
before him, and the Church will dilate upon his
moral beauties, and all unpleasant subjects will
he buried as far as possible out of sight.
Humanity and democratic sentiment might
cause the nation to look coldly on such royal
slaughtermen as Coffee of Ashantee, or Gelele
of Dahomey, but the heir-apparent of the
Russian throne is an altogether different in-
stitution. In this respect England and Aus-
tralia stand on much the same level. The great
soul of the British nation swelled with indigna-
tion at the tale of the Polish butcheries of 1863,
but when the chief assassin—the lamented Czar
Alexander —visited London a few years later,
nobody, save one half-crazy Pole, dreamt of
refusing to do him honour. And for the sake
of the good name of Poland we remember, with
a species of regret, that that demented exile
was a lamentably bad shot, and thanks to his
nerveless hand a brilliant chance was thrown
away. I* is hardly likely that any such de-
pressing experiment will be repeated in Aus-
tralia, and if the Czarovitcii thinks fit to visit
this country, he may do so with a full con-
viction that nobody will fire at him, or at all
events, that nobody will fire straight enough
to do any damage. And supposing any
ruined outcast or expatriated Nihilist should
manifest signs of a disloyal turn of mind, it is
refreshing to think that an Act will promptly
be passed providing for the imprisonment of
everybody who refuses or neglects to take off
his hat and cheer whenever a Sovereign is men-
tioned, or whenever he comes across the name
of one in print, and to provide for the condign
punishment of everybody who fails to manifest
signs of boundless enthusiasm when he hears of
a monarch, either directly or indirectly under
any circumstances whatever. It is one of the
melancholy signs of human instability that the
Democracy, in most cases, is only democratic
when there is no use for it. It despises
monarchy and aristocracy in general and grovels
to the individual monarch or aristocrat at every
opportunity ; it 'prizes human liberty and it
sends a contingent to crush out liberty in the
Soudan, and having repented that insane
manoeuvre in dust and ashes, it is liable to
repeat the experiment in its next outburst of
popular frenzy, and to be disgusted at itself
afterwards for doing so. And, consequently, if
a Grand Duke should obtrude himself in Aus-
tralia there is no doubt that he will be received
with banquets and illuminations, and that he
will bo liailed as the grandest and noblest of
humanity : and the half-brick of obloquy and
contempt will only be heaved at his memory
after he is gone. But let us trust that the story
is not true, and that it will be his blessed
privilege to stay away.

Concerning Bastardy.
The kind attention of the numerous .fol-dimnt
purists who snuffle against any relaxation of
the law as regards illegitimacy, and believe
in letting the sins of the fathers descend
upon the “ offspring of sha-ame,” is hei’e-
by called to the fact that one Sir Richard
Wallace, Bart., has just passed hence in the
odour of sanctity and the love of his Sovereign
Lady, at the age of 72. Sir Richard Wallace
was what would be unctuously described as a
“bastard” if he hadn’t been a baronet. The
only things to distinguish Richard from the
common or garden kind of “bastard”—the poor,
scraggy, “ bye-blow ” with a neglected nose—
were the ghastly wickedness and iniquitous
wealth of his reputed papa, the fourth Marquis
of Hertford. If anyone here present has
fallen so far from grace as to read Thackeray’s
“Vanity Fair,” he will please to understand
that old Hertford was faithfully depicted in the
character of that revolting libertine, the most
noble the Marquis of St ey ne, and that one among
the myriads of Becky Sharers who ministered
to that cultured beast’s taste for debauchery was
the vehicle by which Sir Richard Wallace
arrived in this censorious world. When
“ Steyne ” Hertford died, some 20 years ago,
he left the major portion of his vast possessions
to this favoured “accident,” who was then
promptly created a baronet and K.C. B. in osten-
sible recognition of certain lavish gifts of money
and art-treasures which it pleased or suited
him to bestow upon the Nation. Dignified
clergy sat around Ins festive hoard and paid
homage to the notoriousreprohate’shastard sou—
the Archbishop of Canterbury was glad of any op-
portunity to shake his revered forefinger, Hehoh-
uohbcd with royalty, just as did the sporting
Hen ry Saville, illegitimate son and heir of the
late Earl of Stradroke. The purists who would
Hing “ the disgrace of his birth ” in the teeth of
any man born of Miss Mary, the house-
maid, through the agency of an impecunious
baker, acquiesced in that social law which re-
cognises tlie superiority of a noble’s bastard who
get the property over the “ rightful heir ” who
has little save his hereditary title to keep him
going Nothing, shrieks professional piety, can
wash off' the stain of his parents’ sin from
Mary's “nameless” child, and to let him stand
upon an equality with subsequent offspring "f
that guilty pair—offspring produced with the
sanction of the “ Church ” —were a direct
encouragement to immorality, urges the
parson. Observe how effectually the same
old stain can he gilded o’er by the gold
of a dissolute marquis, or earl, who never
atoned for, nay, almost justified, the in-
dulgence of a natural instinct by joining with
hig paramour in the holy bonds of matrimony.
What was Sir Richard Wallace’s baronetage
hul a royal encouragement to immorality, ae-
cording to the teachings of narrow-minded
bigots? How ready are said bigots to pocket
their scruples, and fawn upon such influential
“bastards” when they have a chance! The
cold-blooded cruelty of attempts to put a brand
of infamy upon the innocent children of un-
married parents is not more, obvious than the
hollowness and insincerity of that “morality”
which allows the rank and money of the male
progenitor to set aside a so-called religious
principle. We take the case of Wallace, not
by way of reflecting discredit upon the dead
man, but as a specially suitable sneer at the
Pharisees who profess their burning anxiety to
put “ illegitimate” children beyond the pale of
society. Sir R. Wallace, Bart., toiled not,
neither did lie spin. He was remarkable for
nothing but the wealth which was the result of
his unhallowed birth, and for the culchaw placed
within his reach by the whim of one infamous
as “the wickedest man in Christendom.” So
he died honoured and mourned by a virtuous
Queen and church, and the distressing tidings
of his demise were cabled over to Australia,
where nice old gentlemen like the Dean of Mel-
bourne are just now discussing the “ stain of
illegitimacy,” and endeavouring to rub it into
household Mary’s little lamb.

A Happy Thought.
Coroner Ygtjl, of Melbourne, urges that a
Bill be passed commanding “that all un-
married women, anticipating maternity, shall
be compelled under severe penalties to
register themselves ” as a protection to their
offspring. A measure of an almost equally
stringent character is already before the Vic-
torian Assembly, but the unreasoning scare
which has arisen through the constant discovery
of dead infants in all manner of unexpected
places is leading the ‘ ‘ religious ” section of the
community to clamour for some tremendous
scheme of repression, and the coroner’s idea about
fills the Bill. Whenever anything goes wrong
in Australia the first idea of the governing
powers is to pass an Act demanding that the
person supposed to be responsible shall
register himself, and if things are very far
wrong the delinquent is generally required to
fill up a large blue form in addition, and deposit
it with some public functionary. If the case
is an absolutely desperate one provision is
also made that somebody shall inspect
something at certain intervals, and send
hi a report which nobody ever reads. The
necessity for finding out the cause and the pos-
sible remedy of a great evil is altogether subor-
dinate to the necessity for having that evil
“ inspected” and having an immense document
drawn up concerning it, and if the world is full
of misery it is much more important that that
misery should be entered and initialled in a big,
dog-eared volume than that it should be reme-
died. But while the coroner is on the job we
would be glad to know why he doesn’t insist
that the intending embezzler should also be
required to register himself before going off with
the cash, and why'the larrikin isn't to be in-
spected before he goes out and commits a brutal
assault. The results are likely to be about as
good in one case as the other. The girls who
had been led astray would nearly all evade
registration —the very fact that child-birth
would lay them open to a legal penalty and
public exposure, would make them ten times
more anxious than ever to risk and procure
abortion. Dr. Yotl threatens to be no more
successful in social science than the gimlet-hole
squi liters of the Social Purity Society. He starts at
the wrong end when lie tries to flatten out human
nature by the steam-roller of cut-and-dried law.
Without any possible desire to cast doubts on
the sanctity of human life, whether in infants
or coroners, we would be glad to know if Dr.
Yotl is prepared to do anything to make it
worth while for the kind of infant in question
to remain alive instead of being found dead in
a creek or a coal-box. To live, and be shunted
into a baby-farm, and be found a few years
later sleeping in a boiler or on a doorstep and
run in as a neglected child, and then to be
brought up in a reformatory, and to develope
afterwards into an “unemployed,” or to catch
an incurable cold doing pick-and-shovel work on
the roads, and to wind up by spending a joyous
old age in the Benevolent Asylum is not a sub-
ject for healthy ambition, and it is just possible
that many of the class whom the coroner yearns
over may live to wish that they hadn’t been
kept alive by quite so much registration and
“ inspection ” in the days of their cheerless
in fancy.

A Vegetable Wanted.
The University of Melbourne has risen in
majestic dudgeon, because one of its professors
the irrepressible .lenks —lately delivered a
speech in advocacy of the opening of libraries
and museums on Sunday. To quote the
language of an unspeakably solemn doctor with
a pained expression on his face, the Council feels
that “ it is intolerable that any section of the
community should have its opinions, or even its
prejudices, flouted by a salaried officer of the
University,” and under Ihose circumstances
•lenks is duly warned that he must never again
be found hollering in the van of any progressive
idea, or attempting to do good to his fellow
creatures. It is true that a late professor (Hearn )
of the same institution was allowed to go into
politics and to advocate the cause of “ pro-
putty ” for many years in the Upper House
without hindrance or remonstrance, but pro-
bably there is a difference somewhere, and it is
allowable to be a snag in the Dead Sea of the
Council, while it is absolutely disgraceful to be
a radical and a reformer in the Town Hall.
Still there doesn’t seem to be much inducement
to a. Melbourne professor under the new r<'<ihne.
If he isn't to sit on anybody's opinions or
prejudices he must lie a carrot rather than
a man, for no man ever lived who could agree
with everybody, and a man who is worth any-
thing to humanity generally starts by disagree-
ing with the views of most of the human race.
If lie is to contradict nobody then he can teach
nothing, for the one thing he is allowed to say
is what all the world knows already, and in that
case it is not worth while to say it. And if the
first requisite of a professor is that he must have
trodden on himself to such an extent as to have
lt>: I all do ire to contradict anybody, or to lake
part in any public movement, or to do any good
to humanity, lest he should injure the feelings
of somebody who didn’t want it done, then a
great difficulty will be experienced in finding
any sufficiently dull and soulless clod to fill the
bill. Even the worm, or the Rev. Robert
Spalding, will turn on an emergency, and as
the Melbourne professor is to be a person who
never turns at all, it is evident that he must be
humbler and flatter than either of these. And
when the requisite clod has been discovered what
good will he be after all ? A teacher is supposed
to be something better and more active than a
locomotive corpse crowded-up with Greek roots,
or a hollow image filled with participles ; and
if he isn’t he had better be replaced by a phono-
graph or by an automatic figure warranted to
speak when a penny is dropped into the slot.
As for the contention that the dignity of the
professor’s office should prevent him meddling
with matters relating to the common, outside
herd, it can only be replied that when any man
grows too dignified to be an ordinary human
being he had better resign his position in the
Caucasian race, and assume his rightful atti-
tude on all-fours. And when next a vacancy
takes place in the University of Melbourne we
would suggest that the Council should honestly
define the situation by inserting the brief but
comprehensive advertisement —• “Vegetable
wanted,” else some day it might find itself in-
volved in an unexpected charge of obtaining a
vertebrate animal under false pretences.

Taxation plus Kent.
In the Twentieth Century R. H. Cowdery
states that Henry George’s argument, if sound,
that ‘ ‘ all improvements, of whatsoever nature,
in the surroundings, conditions and abilities of
men, simply increase the rent he must pay to
the landowner,” nullifies any attempt to abolish
poverty by means of a land system that does
not wholly abolish the landlord. For, reason-
ing from this basis, the improvements in a
tenant’s condition resulting from a lessening of
taxation by the imposition of the Single-tax,
would, like other betterments under any system
of landlordism, therefore result in an increase
of rent; and a decrease in taxation that simply
puts money into the pockets of a privileged
class, simply defeats its own object. The
limited form of Single-tax, that which only
intends to confiscate sufficient rent to fulfill
fiscal uses, would, therefore, mean increased
rents, the burden of taxation being superadded
to that of rent. As for the unlimited Single-
tax, supposed to be equal to 90 per cent, of the
whole rental value of land, it would place the
landlords in the position of rent-collectors who
would deduct 10 per cent, from all amounts
collected ere handing them over to the State,
while the enormous sums that would thus come
into governmental hands would require to be
filtered back somehow for the benefit of
the community, leaving behind much of
their richness adhering to the sponges of
office and the treasury octopi. In any case,
the paying over to the State of sums which the
State then returns less wayside absorptions and
the wear-and-tear of Treasury travel, when thus
examined of itself does not seem calculated to
increase comforts and reduce responsibilities.
If George’s scheme be considered according to
his claims as a means to make landlords either
use or relinquish idle land, then Pentecost’s
plan —which would throw all vacant lots open
to the use and occupation of any man who
wants a home or craves an opportunity to toil—
has all the virtues of the Single-taxers’ methods,
none of its evils, and all the loveliness of ex-
treme simplicity.

The Melbourne 'Telegraph issues this sombre
note of warning re the incessant Public Library
problem : ‘ ‘ The occupant of the British Throne
is sworn to uphold a certain religion, of which
the due observance of the Sabbath is one of the
cardinal points ; therefore, Crown officials who
conspire against the sanctity of the Sabbath are
guilty of something like sedition. ” Perhaps so.
On reading this, it seems to us that the “occu-
pant of the British Throne ” has pledged herself
to uphold a thing that is a great deal too
large for her to lift, and that she has placed
herself very much in the position of a fly
that is walking off with a sack of flour.
Also, the “due observance of the Sabbath”
in this case only meant the way the
Telegraph observes the Sabbath, and if the
Telegraph observed the Sabbath just a shade
better that paper couldn’t appear at all on Mon-
thly morning. And further, “ sedition” is only
a comparative affair at the best; in the days
when that offence was a lit tle better defined,
the whole Telegraph staff would have gone to
gaol for publishing Parliamentary reports, and
consequently (his idiotic offence has fallen into
contempt; and as for “conspiracy,” if it is
conspiracy to agitate for the repeal of a Sunday
law, it is also conspiracy to agitate for the
passing of such a statute. And, lastly,
nobody cares a straw whether it is con-
spiracy and sedition or not, and it is
nobody’s affair what the “occupant of the
British Throne ” is pledged to, and that occu-
pant can either uphold the national religion or
let it go according as she feels inclined ; and if
she feels that the religion in question is slipping
she can sand her hands and take a fresh grip,
provided she wants to do so, and the “ obser-
vance of the Sabbath ” may keep on being a
cardinal point or give it up, just as it likes. It
won’t make the slightest- difference to anybody
in Australia.
The Remission of Penalties Bill, introduced by
Attorney-General Simpson into the New South
Wales Parliament, is a milk-and-water measure
apparently conceived in the interests of the
Conservative Press. ft does not abolish the
large penalties recoverable under some musty
English statute or other by any common in-
former who may prosecute a newspaper which
publishes advertisements of Sunday enter-
tainments to which admission is charged,
but it gives the Governor-in-Council dis-
cretionary power to remit any penalties
decreed by the Supreme Court. With
a perfect remembrance of the manner in
which Premier Parkes treated his political
enemies (Proctor. Walker, and so on) by
means of Um-dkerctionary power ve; ted in him
as Colonial Secretary, we regard the present Bill
with a sinister eye. Suppose, for example, that
The Bulletin alone had rendered itself liable
to such penalties, with what energy would a
politician whose minions vainly tried to crush
a Sunday newspaper by devious legal means
have rushed to the rescue ! Ha, ha ! (food
old Sir Henry Pakkes ! The present Bill is
merely a Bill to protect Fairfax and Sons, and
their rivals and imitators, and to prevent Tommy
Walker, the atheistic lecturer and Radical
politician.
That more or less eminent personage, the Earl
of Jersey, who is shortly to be shipped out as
Governor of New South Wales, has aroused the
enthusiasm of the “Liberal” Daily News,
which states that “ his lineage ought to
be sufficient to satisfy the most exacting
colony. ” We presume that Jersey’s ‘ ‘ lineage ”
simply means his ancestors, and, however
good they may have been when they were
alive they are no earthly use now that
they are dead. Besides, unless they are going
to be dug up and shipped out here what in
thunder have they got to do with the case, and
why should N.S. Wales be satisfied with them ?
It doesn’t want to take satisfaction out of any-
body’s dead relatives, and it can’t reasonably
Hit anybody’s ancestor after he is gone. And
if Jersey’s progenitors were first-class people
it is so much the more likely that they used up
all the brains in the family, for intellect
seldom lasts out two consecutive genera-
tions ; and if they were an idiotic lot
it is quite likely that Jersey is of the
same stamp, for brains are not infectious and
the other thing certainly is. The only reason-
able interpretation which we can put on the
News’ theory that New South Wales is to lie
satisfied with Jersey’s lineage rather than with
Jersey himself, is that the dead ancestors are a
better property than their live descendant, but
whether this means that they are bearing up
manfully against their deadness, or that such a
family is’ incapable of making a favourable im-
pression when it is alive, we are unable to
imagine. Possibly the real explanation is that
when the people of Australia inspect the new
Governor they will feel glad that there isn’t any
more of him in the family, and will be satisfied
to hear that the others are extinct, but anyhow
there is very little satisfaction in the paragraph
no matter what view you take of it, and when
you take six different views there is no satis-
faction at all.

Of the late Mr. Dalley, Q.C., that gusiiing
young barrister, J. G. O’Ryan writes :
In a land where he never set foot, and was known only
to fame, and in the greatest and proudest city the world
has seen, he is to-day commemorated by a monumental
tablet in one of England’s most noble cathedrals.
Now Dalley visited England in ’61 — in com-
pany with Parkes —as an Australian emigration-
lecturer. Of the same gentleman, a P.C. by
courtesy, Lord Rosebery says :
Mr. Halley will live in history, not only as a colonial
Minister, blit also as an Imperial statesman.
Rosebery might have made an excellent chair*
man of the London County Council, but his
estimate of Dalley ’s hold on posterity is sadly
exaggerated. As a public orator, Dalley was
but a glorified Dan O’Connor ; as a litterateur,
he produced a few trifles, evidently so highly
polished by continual revision that all the virtue
had gone out of them. As a conversationalist,
he was charmingly reminiscential, as an ex-
temporaneous after-dinner speaker, he had no
equal in Australia ; but lie had no claim
to statesmanship. As The Bulletin has
already pointed out, his Soudan bid for
notoriety, however gratifying it may have been
to British ideas of the unity of the Empire, was
merely a useless expenditure of money, and in
the end alienated from him much of the venera-
tion with which he was previously regarded.
No drink is sold in the prohibition town of
Pullman, 11.5., says Rev. F. B. Boyce, and
there is no crime there nor any lock-up. Human
nature must have completely changed at Pullman.
If the town is without a lock-up where do they
put the gluttonous deacons and elders who get into
trouble with the girls at choir-practice, and where
do they locate the smug and oily moonfaced persoft
with the mutton-chop whiskers and long black
coat who fraudulently converts and embezzles the
estates of the widow and orphan? You can’t
wipe out the fact that in all countries there exist
certain special “pious” vices, such as seduction
accompanied by prayer, and trustorial sneak-
thiefery in the name of the Lord—mean things
done on the quiet and on the strength of the greasy
criminal’s good character. For instance, in Aus-
tralia bigamy is a peculiarly holy vice, with a
strong affinity for chemical lemonade and other
such hogwash; and it is the attorney who is a
(Sunday-school superintendent who usually steals
off in the dead of night without leaving any ad-
dress which would appeal to the credulity of
Detective-Inspector Oamphin.

Paul du Chaillu writes a timely article, in
the Fortnightly Review, on Central Africa. He
was the first to penetrate into the region now
described by Stanley. This was a quarter of a
century ago, and he had to stand the brunt as a
Munchausen, with his gorilla, and what not.
Since then he has been an Arctic explorer, with Ids
book on the “ Land of the Midnight Sun.” He
praises Stanley, but brackets him with his com
panions.
The Age has got itself into a rather deep
hole by printing a yarn to the effect that the
Yankee Consul in Melbourne had fled from a £406
debt. Next day the Consul turned up smiling at
his office and informed the inevitable interviewer
—not necessarily for publication but as a guarantee
of good faith—that he had been on view at
Government House for some hours during the
time of his alleged “disappearance.’ A writ for
libel was, at latest dates, in process of manufac-
ture, and David Syme was enquiring how the
something these things find their way into the
paper. According to spicy rumour, an unreliable
jade, by the way, there was a lady at the bottom
of this trouble.

84C Macquarie-street North, Sydney, August 7, 1890.
My Dear Moorabisda,—
The second July “At Home” at Government House on
31st ult. was a purely hansom-cab function, since the
city roads were liquid mud ; nevertheless, the “ red draw-
ing room” was well filled. Lady Carington’s dress was a
dahlia-poplin skirt with bows and ends of moire ribbon,
Figaro jacket in dahlia velvet bordered with gold galon
over a pleated vest of white silk. Mrs. Burdekin appeared
in purple silk and velvet combined with panels of black
chenille fringe and Zouave of the same ; bonnet in velvet
silk and jet, and black parasol, .Miss Walenn wore Indian
red—hut as the room was very dark indeed it really didn’t
much matter what people “ had on.” Very little patronage
was accorded the verandah—the visitors stayed inside
and gushed over the superb camellias.
The Sydney creche, at 21 Macquarie-street South, and
run under the patronage of several philanthropic ladies,
was to have been set afloat by Lady Carington on 29th
ult., hut owing to unforeseen circumstances the ceremony
was indefinitely postponed. The creche is an eight-roomed
house, three storeys high, where children will be well
taken care of during the hours their mothers are at work
—supposed to lie from six a.m. to nine p.m. (a most
liberal allowance of labour, you will say), on the stroke of
which last hour every cherub will be expected to vacate
the premises. Every child will be undressed and bathed
on arrival and re-dressed in a cosy frock belonging to the
creche—Hie.donations of baby-wear have been extremely
liberal - and the children will he fed at inter-
vals on mutton - broth and oatmeal. No child
will be allowed to remain on the premises during the
night, but the upper rooms are filled with cradles and
cots for the youngsters to take their necessary naps. The
fee is only sixpence per day—as at the Brisbane creche,
which is said to he in a very flourishing condition. 1 may
he wrong, hut I cannot help t hinking that the institution
will in a short while find itself with many infants left on
its bands, and that it will ultimately develops into a
foundling hospital—the only real palliative of infanticide.

Believers who follow the gifted couple from city to city

It’s easier for a camel to cat a haystack than for a
presentation to happen in Sydney without a fiasco occur-
ring immediately on the heels of it. Only fifty guineas
rif the eighty due for the gorgeous trophy presented to
somebody'the oilier day were paid to the promoters, and
the climax was a heated person rushing wildly round with
a long blue hill, and only prevented with difficulty from
thrusting it at the victim of the presentation at an awful
moment. What is the usual “testimonial?” Merely the
wish or fad of one enthusiastic or interested man or
woman inflicted upon the indifferent multitude.

“ beauty ” amateur travelling on Hie bridge of her nose
or the narrowness of her smile-a thing Miss Aohurch
disdains to be, of course An admirer of Tolstoi, a
disciple of Ibsen, a woman who thinks for herself and
doesn’t bore you with borrowed reflections on the difficult
social questions peculiar fo our era, that is our
Mrs. Helnier out of harness, with a very small baby
of most unsocial instincts—named Nora, after Hie
Ihsariian heroin?. Mr. and Mrs. Charrington are pleased
at the appreciation of Sydney audiences, and hope to play

" A Lady of the Sea ” (ending happily—according to the
gallery idea of happiness, that is) may he staged. During
the present season the hone of contention, “A Doll’s
House " (only taken off because Miss Acliurch cannot play
the pari 100 long without a rest) will he revived for a few
nights. Mrs. Gharrington’s pet parts are Nora Helnier
and Lady Macbeth, which latter she would like to play
for Sydney's benefit on her return. From Sydney the
Charringtons go to Tasmania (where they will not play
“ A Doll's House ”) and N.Z., and are due in London by
September, 1891.

A MIDDLE AGED Lady with means and comfortable
A home i»iv* * vacancy for middle-aged gentleman
hoarder with small means, views to matrimony.
Why not? How belter to discover whether Romeo’s taste
in the way the eggs should he Ixriled coincides with youis,
or whether his liking for pickled onions jumps with
Juliet’s; than to have him tame on the premises as a
stipendiary hoarder—before marriage,? Lots of connubial
shipwrecks would have been avoided if Torvald would
lodge with Nora's papa for a year—and when you reflect
that in the average marriage a man marries a girl because
She has curly hair or a killing smile, though her method
of cooking cabbage, for all he knows of her, may be
to put it into cold water —then one sees that mar-
riage is indeed a lottery. That middle-aged lady isn’t
going to leap in the dark. She wants, ere she finally
commits herself, to criticise the bouquet of that middle-
aged gentleman’s remarks when he comes home from a
“ lodge-meeting” at two a.m. She wants to see how he
looks in his shirt-sleeves howling for shaving-water on
Sunday morning, also what emotional effect a late dinner
or a hurst boot-lace has upon him before she gives her
happiness Inio his keeping and quite right, too. The
hoarder-suitor is a deliciously practical innovation—if
only society will stand him. And here you are, in the
Herald;
A HOME, in every sense of the word, offered to
- ‘ elderly lady and gentleman ; no young children,
no husband, no hoarders.

Ever since Inaugural Arbor Day at Ryde there has been
a perfect epidemic of spades, little treelets in potlets,
and general holidays at public schools, while everybody
plants a tree so that posterity may find itself possessed of
at least one realisable asset in the shape of firewood.
Wavcrlcy Public School avborated on Ist instant in the
presence of a crowd of local people and a peppering o
M’s.L.A., headed by the member for Paddington, who
broke into poetry at the end of his humourous speech,
after which everybody agreed it was time for refreshment,
which was daintily set out in a class-room and partaken
Of by the invited guests. Mrs. Carruthers, wife of the
Minister of Public Instruction, in a black gown and
jacket with crape revers, perpendioularised the first
shrub, fol'owed by Mrs. Alfred Allen (one of the lady
members of the local hoard', who wore a handsome dark
green silk gown with black velvet and passementerie
Visite and bonnet in green and cream tints. The pro-
ceedings concluded with the inspection of the cadets, and
tree-planting by Mrs. Guest (Mayoress) and other ladies.

If Miss Acliurch stages Ibsen’s “ Lady of the Sea” in
Sydney, there will ho a great backsliding among the little
company of believers in the Norwegian G.0.M., I know.
For the plot is too clear. A young Viking mashes a
maritime maiden—flings Hie “ engagement” ring into tire
mottled deep, with Hie spiteful remark that she can’t
“break it off” now unless she hires a diving-suit, and
then fades away arid forgets to come back until the girl
marries a soap-boiler, or something capable of eating
three square meals daily. How the Viking is of
opinion that Hie marriage-certificate is as naught to the
rusty ring in the raging main as a matrimonial guarantee;
how the Ibsenish wife is at first of the Viking’s opinion,
and is distracted with doubt until a surprisingly rational
• moment arrives when she fires the Viking out and cleaves
to the soap-boiler, will ho so much gall and wormwood
to some of the feminine disciples of “A Doll s House,”
who would rather the old man got his “deserts,” of course.
The Halles have occasion to he humbly thankful to an
individual who shall he nameless, lately connected with
their concert-tour. To him in Sydney one day came an
excited parent, who wanted to he t vd how he could burst
in upon Sir Charles at the head of a gesticulating deputa-
tion of other excited parents and implore him to preside
at a monster matinrie where all the kids who hang the
piano or Scrape the fiddle should play their best—the
Knight and his lady to deliver judgment at the finish!
It was a nobly selfish idea—actuated, probably, by the
fact that the originator’s Mariar could play “Alice” rings
round the minx next door, who took on airs, and he
wanted to squeeze the conceit out of her at once. However,
owing to the strategy of the person referred to in the
beginning, the deputation didn’t wait. Let other distin-
guished musicians beware.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Sydney Reunions held the last hut one of their well
managed dances at West’s rooms on Ist August, when,
owing perhaps to its I icing Bank holiday, the attendance
was hut moderate. The dresses were very pretty in some
instances, a gown in white satin with tulle waterfall,
the front draped with tulle, dotted with clusters of lilies
of the valley ; a mauve silk made perfectly plain ; and a
dress in shades of tan in silk, moire and fisher's net, look-
ing very well indeed. But one or two fearsomely baggy
old-fashioned “Greek” garments, only tolerable when
they were “in,” suggested all manners of things. The
refreshments were nice, as usual, and floor and music
extremely good. The Reunions will not be held during
next season.
Woollahra private assemblies held another hop at
Nithsdale on Ist hist., when the fun was fast and furious,
as usual. An appealing toilette was in ecru armure
draped with crystal net and clusters of deep red roses;
lavender crepe de chine in lamp shade pleats over an
underdress of panSy silk, was also very pretty. The music
by Need’s band was excellent.

A story has been going the rounds of the
press lately of a Hungarian woman who kept her-
self within doors for thirty years, as a self-inflicted
punishment for going out to a fair after promising
her sweetheart that she would not do so. Strange
as it may seem, that narrative has its parallel in a
prosaic suburb of Sydney. Let those who are in-
clined to indulge in a little sentiment take the
Newtown tram, and when opposite the skating-rink
a little distance this side of the Missenden-road
they will see above a large hoarding the roof of a
house, in which for 34 years a woman kept herself
a prisoner, watching and waiting for a lover who
never returned. Some 37 years ago Miss Flora D.
was a beautiful and wealthy girl, residing with
her father in that old-fashioned house. She was
engaged to be married to a man who has since
risen to high rank and is now in England, if hs
bo alive. One night, in Miss D’s home, they
had a quarrel over the usual weighty mat-
ters that set lovers by the cars, and Mr. N.
as he was going out remarked, “ Flora, when this
door closes on me to-night it closes on me for
ever.” Miss D. caught the door ere he could shut
it and called him back in vain, for he came no
more. She-stopped at the door all night saying,
“The door must nob bo closed, lie will return.”
Next day, seeing the futility of all entreaties to
her to come away, her father agreed to have the
door fixed so that it could not be closed. A car-
penter was called in, the fastenings adjusted, and
so it remained year in and year out that the par-
able of the foolish virgins might not be again ful-
filled. Another doorway had to be cut at the side
of the house, and the front door was never used
again. Some three years ago Miss D. died, aged
about 58, but up to the day of her death if she
heard any noise in the front a servant was des-
patched at once to see whether Mr. N. had come,
or if anyone was meddling with the door. The
house has been deserted lor some time, but it
stands to-day a mute witness to the fidelity and
patience of a foolish, tender-hearted woman.

We struggled up the Hill of Difficulty
which leads to Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall, the othet
evening, against an outgoing stream of Young,
and, probably, Christian Gentlemen, from whom
emanated a religious aroma, in some eases tem-
pered by a soupron of whisky-u hilf. Alt Hb of
these Youpg Christian Gentlemen Moiled with
difficulty, and others, remembering that they were
mere earthly worms, seem to lay themselvts out
for being trodden on, but vainly. The summit
was attained at an early hour, and so we stood on
the mat among the greenery like a latter-day
serpent in a modern Eden to watch the
tide of fashion and beauty wriggling upstairs.
A tide did we say ? Almost an Irish sea,
for so strongly was it tinctured with Hibernians
that it suggested the Charge of Fontcnuy, an
item fortunately omitted from Miss Dargon’s pro-
gramme of Friday last. Proceedings were opened
by tbe string-band of the N.S. W. Artillery of
whom it was remarked by a journalistic cynic in
our vicinity that they Avero not players but com-
posers. Improvisation when a collective effort
has decided disadvantages. The recitals of Miss
Augusta Dargoh were, as of yore, full of sustained
vigour and line drapaatic contrast, though her
voice has, to some extent, parted company with its
earlier flexibility, but that may have been due to
the weather. Nevertheless, in everything she did
the artist was manifest, though her recitals
throughout, chiefly tragical, partook somewhat
of the lugubrious, particularly in the selection
from Tennyson's “ Queen Mary.” Miss Ameris,
known in Sydney chiefly by her appearance in
“The Yeomen of the Guard,” sang two songs with
a fuller voice than she formerly produced, and
Bandmaster Devery, in a clarionet solo, showed a
perfect mastery over the instrument, though sug-
gesting, somehow, Maccabe’s burlesque, in which
the “ pore toon is worried to death by the waria-
shun.” Miss Minina Paige, a pupil of Mine.
Charbonnet-Kellerman, played a pianoforte solo
in a manner which was not calculated to make
Halle tremble. Throughout the Young Christian
Gentlemen voluntarily supplied an Indian Club
obligato from the rear of the stage. Manager
Stopford was in front, and informs us that these
recitals will be continued should public patronage
warrant.

The awfully moral persons'who objected
so strenuously to Nora’s desertion of her husband
for'“a mere whim,” having approved of, or at
least failed to denounce the conduct of Gilberte,
who forsakes her husband for the arms of a
younger man, business has not been so good at
Sydney Criterion during the “Frou Frou” run as
in that of “A Doll’s House.” The performance
all round has greatly improved on the opening
night’s, and is doubtless in advance of any repre-
sentation of French emotional comedy seen here
for years. “Fedora” will probably be staged in
the middle of next week.

Despite cold, rain, wind, hail at Sydney
Royal, good houses prevail. The excellent stage-
management of “ Paul Jones,” thanks to Johnny
Wallace, is a thing of universal comment; in fact,
no one seems to growl except some ladies who
consider that Tenor Wood field, who looks lovely,
hasn't a fair show. We prefer Marian Burton’s
decollete notes to her high necked efforts, which
are sometimes a little “chinny,” but- Nellie
Stewart seems always to produce the proper key
from her private bunch, though she might resp ml
to encores with a better grace. The lady who
says nightly, without stop or accentuation, “ A-
duel-never-the-ambush-is-laid,” or something to
that effect, always reminds us of the cld-time
Sydney hash-house waitress with her “Roast-beef-
boiled-mutton, &c., vvhat-your’s ?” monotony, and
we always feel disappointed when Snazelle fails to
order duel on toast, and Leitch ambush with
onions. As the management are advertising for
tall and shapely young ladies, we presume “ Boc-
caccio ” is in active preparation.

Harry Rickards re-opened at million prices
on Saturday night last at Sydney School of Arts
to one of the largest houses ever crammed therein.
The Co. has been reconstructed for the better
since last visit. Ada Walker, almost unknown
to Sydney audiences, has a sweet, cultured
voice. Mrs. Rickards plays the slavey quietly
and effectively in an adaptation of “Turn Him
Out,” in which ’Arry enacts a coster to the life.
Rickards brings several new songs along ; and one
of Godfrey’s sketches, “ Seeing Life,” is certain to
be popular.
-;if * *
The unexpected illness of Maggie Moore,
first made known to the management of Her
Majesty’s on Saturday at 1 p.m., would have
closed that building, for the evening at all events,
had .things been in less vigorous hands. But
messengers were sent hither and thither, the Meg
s.t nery and properties were stowed away and
others substituted, a rehearsal given at 4 o’clock,
and precisely at 8 the curtain rang up on “After
Dark” to a kindly audience, not prepared in all
cases, despite the hanging out of notices, for the
change. “ After Dark” will be run until “The
Corsican Brothers” is quite ready for production,
unless the management deem it advisable to revive
some old favourite for a fortnight or so. Meantime
the piece will be livened up by the engagement of
the best talent on hand for the music-hall scene,
in which Baby Nicholls, Arthur Rigby, “Bill
Stevings of Bonding,” Chucky Osborne, the
dancer, Billy Warner, and others take part.

bird-warbling of the manager concealed behind
the canvas.

The Bijou alone, of all the Melbourne
theatres, rejoiced in a crowded attendance last
Saturday. “ Kerry ” and “ The Balloon,” which
together constitute a full bill with a wide sweep,
are evidently destined to repeat the Sydney success
of a few months back, every pigeon on the ground
being safely grassed with one or other of B. and
lb s barrels. It is particularly noticeable that the
superior, but under-dressed females, who cannot
weep over “Kerry” because their backs are too
chilly, laugh themselves into a warm glow by th -
time* Brough has descended from his brief visit b»
the heavens above. In truth, “The Balloon” is
brighter and more human in its absurdity than
any farce lately produced in these parts, notwith-
standing that” both plot and dialogue have a
chestnut flavour, and about the performance
there is a wild freshness that does infinite credit to
a company whose personalities have become so
familiar in a succession of pieces running on the
same light lints. But for some occasi nallapses
into exaggerated buffoonery, Brough’s sketch of
the victimised doctor is almost as acceptable as
Anton’s finished picture of Eitzjohn, a character
strongly suggestive of the “Rocket.” Miss
Homer is incisively droll, as usual, and for thex-est
Mrs. Brough, Lyndal, Emma Temple and Pattie
Browne, play parts well within their grip in a
manner that—as the daily journal delights to ob-
serve— “ leaves nothing to be desired.” They arc
quite nice enough to eat, with the male exception
of Lyndal, and even he, being a cheerful, seraphic
curate kind of young man, might make a satis-
factory meal for many hungry virgins, if be were
curried.
Boucrcault’s performance of Kerry—in
which he is handicapped (by the penalty attach-
ing to youth) above his grand, giddy progenitor,
and J. C. Williamson —revives recollections of the
ill-fated French company who played the original
of Dion B.’s comedy at Exhibition time. As com-
pared with a foreign gentleman who was eternally
toeing the footlights to jabber his joys and sorrows
at the stalls, Boncioault the younger is great all
the time. At the last, when his legs collapse and
his overwrought frame begins t > fall asunder, tbe
genuine pathos of the situation moves even the
dress circle to a display of enthusiasm. Mrs.
Brough, as the wife restored to the arms of a
drowned husband, is best when the strain upon
her powers is greatest, and the others are good
enough. The present programme should remain
in full blast until part of the company go Sydney-
wards, in two or three weeks’ time.
“ Held by the Enemy,” as set up at the
Opera House, is richer in gunpowder than in
genius. When the supreme interest of a story is
centred in a corpse, suddenly disclosed to view
upon a stretcher, the gloom requires to be relieved
by brighter light comedy than Miss Madge Sey-
mour and Greenaway can rise to. The latter
seems natural enough at times, but the young
lady’s artificial vivacity is more gruesome than the
aforesaid dead body. Equally trying is Miss Mel-
ville's undignified conception’ of a maiden aunt,
while Miss Watts-Phillips becomes woefully stagey
after a pretty love-scene with Inman,in the first act.
In point of fact, the company supporting William
Rignold are not equal to the strong work before
them, and even of the “ star's” vigorous perform
ance it will probably be said that the truth is not
in it. Rignold’s idea of an army-surgeon seems to
have-been grafted on old Jack Saxton, and tb •
suspicions' sawbones is noisy and choleric where t >
most minds lie should appear cold-blooded an I
soldier-like. Anyway, bis big scene with ilo
stretcher created only a moderate effect on Satur-
day night, nor did the drama exert a paralysing
influence at any other point.
A revival of “La Tosca,” at Melbourne
Princess’, failed to crowd the house where it ran
four weeks on the lirst production. The present
performance naturally shows a slight improve-
ment in the direction of Mrs. Potter, and when
that interesting artiste can be induced to tone
down her right arm, and put her smile under
restraint, she will wear the mantle of Sara Bern-
hardt without finding it so many sizes too large.
As a masterpiece of dramatic art “La Tosca”
must command sufficient attention (albeit times
are phenomenally bad in Melbourne) until Satur-
day, when “ Frou-Frou ” is expected to fetch up
the rank and fashion in large quantities.
* * *- *
“ Formosa” has sinned and repented to
disheartening business, at the good old Royal,
where the sweet little flower of success has left off
blooming for many a night. The old doctor, and
Airs. Boker, not to mention sundry other
calamities, have proved too much for “Formosa,”
though the warmth recently infused into the name-
part by Miss Edith Blande ought to make a
bright red spot appear on the top of every bald
head in the city. George Darrell purposes fling-
ing himself into the deadly breach next Saturday
(oth) with his latest hair-raiser, “ The Lucky
Lot.” A lot of luck is just what the theatre
wants badly.
*■ * * *
At the Alexandra, too, Bland Holt intends
to run up “The Golden Ladder” on a scale of
splendour, and “ Master and Man,” which has
outlasted two contemporary productions, is now
on the verge of retirement. Such a show, at such
prices, is seldom or never seen hereabouts, and the
renowned Alexandra gallery is a greater site for
the gods than ever The Victoria Hall still keeps
its capable nigger-crowd going, while the pros-
perity of the Gaiety, under Slade Murray’s man
agement, has been enhanced by an alleged mis-
creant, who made an unsuccessful attempt to
burn it out of existence last Sabbath morning. In
this averted tragedy Slade Murray says he detects
the hand of some jealous rival, or arch-enemy of
the State, whose fiendish design was frustrated by
an all-merciful fire-brigade. A shilling admission
fee has turned up trumps for the Phonograph, but
the second season of the Halles, at Melbourne
Town Hall, is serving to expose the hollowness of
any boom in classical music. Sassiety has “ turned
it up” to a considerable extent, and the Knight
and his Lady are discovering that a bogus taste
for sonatas is apt to come and go, like a swarm of
locusts, while the people really educated up to
severe music expect to go in on the classical nod.
4$ $ $
Alfred Danipier has just flashed above the
horizon with a very significant advertisement to
the effect that he holds the sole rights of “drama-
tisation and representation ” of sundry Australian
literary works, notably “ Robbery Under Arms,”
and “The Miner’s Right.” It is vaguely sus-
pected that somebody or other might, could,
would, or shouldn’t desire to jump Dampier’s
lawful claims, so the advt. is perhaps intended to
lighten the moral darkness of such individuals
and bring them face to face with the awful conse-
quences appertaining to any nefarious scheme of
the description hinted at. If any persons decline
to accept this solemn warning, the damages will
be upon their own heads.

Premier Morehead, of Queensland, lately
shut down—from conscientious motives —on a
Sunday balloon ascent at Charters Towers. You
can get up a first-class dog-fight at the Towers any
Sunday yon like, and sool the two animals on till
nothing remains of them but one ear and half-a-
tail; also you can cuss, and pfiy euchre with two
bowers hidden down your back and an ace in your
boots ; and you can use strong language in the
street, and get up a mining swindle, and take-in
the widow and fatherless over a wild-cat claim on

Geelong is waking up even in the matter of
show business. Charles Hugo was recently lined
20s. and costs for allowing the passages of the local
theatre to be blocked-up by an excited throng of
patrons. For years it has been the dream of
managers’ lives to fix “ standing-room only ” out-
side the Geelong theatre. The few lost sheep who
attended were usually lured in by the inducement
of being able to lie all along the benches.
it- * it it
During the recent production of “After
Dark,” at Melbourne Royal, it was at first ar-
ranged that a super should act as double to Grade
Warner and make the plunge into the water, but
that the rescue should be done by Charles Warner
himself. But the “ eminent” one was not going
to catch cold over it. Certainly not. His life
was much too valuable to be lost to the Australian
stage. Arrangements were accordingly made for
heating the water to such a pitch that it could be
bathed in with some feeling of personal comfort.
Even then it was not without a good deal of cogi-
tation as to the possibility of knocking out tin-
bottom of the tank with his head that the great
man made the plunge. Make it he did,
however, and then looked for the reward
of courage in the shape of press-notices.
To his intense disgust, however, more than on-
critic announced that a “made-up” super, tod.
Charles Warner’s place for this particular- hid
dent. This was more than Warneresque human
nature could bear, and he promptly “chucked’’
the jumping business from that day. The unfor-
tunate fifteen-shilling-a-week man, who was told
off to make the plunge in his place, found, how-
ever, that hot baths were meant for his master,
and had the pleasure of sousing himself every
night in water pretty well down to freezing-point,
the heating apparatus being now “ quite unneces-
sary, deah boy.”

Frank Thornton finished up his season
at the Adelaide Royal with “Mamma” and the
trial scene, Bardell v. Pickwick. To assist in the
latter, a number of local celebrities were engaged.
The Rev. Robert Spalding takes his ecclesiastical
form on to the silver city, Broken Hill, and then
proceeds to England in search of further novelties.
“The Yeomen of the Guard” follow him in
Adelaide where good business is expected to be
done. Fanny Liddiard is a prime favourite in
the m ning-boom city, and Elton, Vernon,
Grundy and Co. are all well liked, while a hearty
reception awaits the new comers, Clara Merivale
and the baritone, Imano.

Whoever.thought to see “Formosa” again? In jfcl-
houriie, 20 yeaVs ago, the cast included Hoskins as Major
Jorum, Mrs. Crosby as Mrs. Hokcr, and Leopold as the
Hobby, in which he got the biggest round of anybody.
Notice how beautifully spick-and-span the sheet picture-
posters are on the big hoarding opposite where the stars
live. The gentleman star comes out and twiddles his
stick ; the lady star blushingly shunts up her umbrella.
Go to remote places, where they never take their walks
abroad, arid the twenty-sheeters are hanging in rags, after
the rain that has been so useful to the country. The old
pictures come but underneath in a puzzling way. Nicolini
used to go up on a ladder, and measure Patti on the
posters, to see that she was long enough.
The prima donna is dying out as the grand factor of
opera. Tenors are nearly as big, and baritones and bassos
almost. Look at Verdi’s “Otello,” with its stars, Tamagno
the tenor, and Maurel the baritone—a blow to the reign of
Patti. All tliis denotes the’healthy revival of opera.
What a tine Bishop of Sydney the Rev. R. S. Smythe
would have made !
Charlotte Cushman was nigh starving when she got her
Prst London engagement. When Maddox, the manager,
said she could not get an opening, she fell on her knees,
with a mixture of Meg Merrilles and Lady Macbeth, ex-
claiming : “ I have enemies in this country, but, by God,
I will defeat them.” Maddox then fancied her, and she
made her hit in Fazio.
The most shocking part of the Ober-Ammergau play is
where you hear Christ being hammered on to the cross,
behind the scenes This has far more effect on the nerves
even than the act ual sight of the crucifixion, which fol-
lows when the curtain rises. Before the play begins,
every night, or afternoon, the performers all kneel down
for prayer, conducted by the village priest.
Baldwin, of parachute notoriety, lias settled down on
a fortune. He gained his £BO,OOO in two and a half
years. At the Alexandra Palace he received £l5O for
every ascent that he made. This was always paid to him
in solid cash, and he never went up until the two stout
hags containing it had been brought into his tent in the
grounds, where- Madame Baldwin 'divided her thoughts
between them and her intense anxiety for the fate,of her
husband. Baldwin himself neu-r experienced a tremor
m'making his perilous aerial journeys. Qn the evening
of h's fast ascent lie tore his balloon and parachute 'o
rate is, gave a champagne supper to his friends, and it
s-'v td to risk his life no more.

pany at London Lyceum, is said to be the finest pure
comedy actress now on the stage.
Sarasate, the peerless violinist, coming to Australia,
never practises or rehearses. He finds it takes the edge
off his public playing. So he has nothing to do but take
his fiddle out of the case at night and witch the world
with ins bow.

Says St. Stephen's Gazette :—“If Mine. Patti was well
enough to sing at all, she could certainly have sung some
respectable song. It was an insult even to those who
patronise ballad-concerts to get up and warble music-hall
ditties.”
