A Convenient Atavism.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last year I was at Lewis Ponds, and had been
staying at one of the hostelries. As I was about
leaving I found I had on hand three-parts of a
bottle of whisky, and not wishing to take it into
Orange - there 1 passed as a never-touoh-it —I
generously decided to make a present of it to two
men whom I had seen knocking about the pub.
I had not far to look for them —they were yarning
in the long dining-room.
“Well, boys,” said I, “will you have some
whisky ?’
“ Gratias tibi,” said Peter, the elder of the two,
who, though an habitual soaker, was a man of
education and accustomed to season his discourse
with Latin scraps.
“That seems good stuff,” quoth Jock, Peter’s
companion.
“ Daresay you’ll find it so,” said I, leaving them
to their own devices. As it wanted some little
time to the starting of the coach I strolled up the
verandah and round by the side of the house.
Chancing to glance through a rent in the blind, I
saw Peter in the act of sampling the liquor, which
was evidently much to his taste. I had uo diffi-
culty in hearing the conversation that ensued or
in seeing all that happened.
“ Well, old man, I’ll have a try now,’ - said Jock,
rather nettled to see his companion knocking the
cork hard home with his hand.
“ You’re much better without it, my man, much
better.”
“That be blowed for a yarn, the boss gave it to
me as well as to you.”
“ Ah, yes, but lie does not know you so well as
I do —he probably takes you for a drunken sot,
like me. ’
“Sot or no sot, I want ray share of the whisky.”
“You should restrain your desires, Jock, they
arc really not healthy.”
Why don’t you restrain yours then ? It’s easy
for you to talk.”
“ I can’t do it, Jock ; you can and must.”
“ Why can’t you do it as well as me ? I tell you
what, you have a dashed cheek.”
“ Not cheek, Jock, not cheek, call it neurosis.
1 have a neurosis, and a d big one too.”
“What’s the good of shamming sick to me? You
want all the liquor, that’s all about it.”
“ My dear fellow, a man with a neurosis is not
necessarily sick—let me explain. A neurosis is
primarily a condition of nerves —but I see that
does not enlighten you. I’ll go back further. My
grandfather I don’t recollect, but my father I do
remember very well, and a nice pippet he was,
too, for as far back as I can call to mind, he was
an inveterate boozer —drunk every chance he got.
I have no doubt that he inherited from his
father a condition of nerves which predisposed
him to the intoxication habit. He was born, in
fact, with a neurosis, which he sedulously culti-
vated. Sedulously in this instance, Jock, means
systematically, lovingly, cheerfully. Ah, yes, I
see you quite understand. Very well. The old
man had the neurosis, and by all that’s holy, he
developed it tremendously before I was born.
You see he transmitted to me, as an heirloom, the
neurosis of his father, but with added strength.
“ Never mind that, I want my drink,” said
Jock.
“ Don’t interrupt me, my dear fellow—as I was
explaining I was born with a neurosis and cannot
help drinking.”
“ What are you giving us, you darned old liar?
You like it.”
“ Like it! ” chuckled Peter, hugging the bottle
caressingly; “ like it—l should say so. That’s the
neurosis. Nosv you don’t like it.”
Here Jock waved his hand deprecatiugly.
“But,” continued Peter, not minding the in-,
terruption, “ I tell you what will happen, if you
force yourself to like it, or by drinking for com-
pany sake allow the habit to become strong—you
will acquire a neurosis, and, what is worse,
transmit it to your children.”
“ Confound you !‘I have no children, and you
know it. ’
“ Quite so. I speak not of children born, but to
be born. For my part, I shall not marry. I owe
a debt to society not to transmit any neurosis—
with me ends a race of drunkards. Jock, my
friend,” continued Peter, rising and waving his
left-hand, but still embracing the bottle with his
right, “do not, I beseech you, by fostering the
drinking habit, sin against society, sorely injure
your bodily health and ” —darting out the door —
“ ruin your immortal soul. ”
“Well, I’m jiggered !” said Jock, “loughter
known the old dorg would collar it all. ”
HENRY SIEBEL.
In London they lately auctioned a lot of
relics from Sippera, Babylonia. They were clay-
written tablets, about 3000 years old, including
some of business transactions, as, for example, the
record of a law-suit by a large fruit-grower against
a proprietor of boats for losing cargo. From these
tablets it is ascertained that slaves were mort-
gaged, banks kept, and everything went on in a
Collins-street way.
The Duke of Fife, who is grandson-in-law,
or step-uncle, or something of the sort, to her
Gracious, had to appear in a common, beastly
police court and swear the other day, and in order
not to hurt his feelings the Bible was wrapped in a
clean cloth before he kissed it. All sorts of vulgar
ordinary people had been embracing that volume,
dontcherknow, and labourers and washerwomen
had testified on its infected boards, and niggers
had come in contact with it, and drunks and
dubious Asiatics had been using it, and fellows in
the retail coal trade, and fellows who smelt of fish,
and costermongers, and persons in the bone and
old fat industry, and chimney-sweeps, and in-
dividuals fresh from the boiling-down business,
and parties who shovelled garbage off the streets
had all been there in turn ; and though the book
was still good enough for any ordinary witness a
duke couldn’t be expected to approach it in its
naked state, unless he was careful to keep to wind-
ward.
Mary Amlorsou’s wedding is thus de-
scribed :
Miss Mary Anderson can boast of having had the most
exclusive wedding of the season. Not only were the
general public excluded from the little church at Hamp-
stead, but the whole of the wedding party, including the
two registrars and the clergyman, Canon Purcell, only
numbered 14, arid no one else was allowed to enter under
any pretence whatever. It was even said that the keyhole
of the door was stuffed up with paper. Certain it is that
the door was both locked and barred during all the time
of the ceremony. Amongst other extraordinary rumours
which flew about was the strange one that Miss Anderson
had arrived at seven o’clock to early Mass, had
then gone straight to the Presbytery which is
next door to the church —and had had her dress
sent-to her, that she might dress at the priest’s house
and so prevent the public seeing her arrive.
Stranger still, lots of people gave the report credence.
The first little excitement was the arrival of the bride-
groom and his brother—the best man—in a hansom. The
two brothers are very much alike, plainly showing their
Spanish origin in their dark skin, hair, and eyes. The
bridegroom, Antonio de Navarro, is not tall, and, though
the difference is not much, he looks a trifle shorter than
his bride. They refused to face the crowd, and went into
the church by the priest’s house. The next to arrive was
the carriage containing the little half-sister and half-
brother of Miss Anderson, the boy in terra-cotta velvet,
Directoire coat,- and knee-breeches, with a white
waistcoat; the girl in white muslin, with deep scar-
let velvet knots, and a scarlet flower at her waist.
They were accompanied by Miss Anderson’s maid, the
only spectator who was not a relation, with the exception
of eight little orphanage boys, who strewed flowers down
the aisle. Then came the bride’s brother and his wife,
the latter in brown, closely followed by Hr. and Mrs.
Griffin, the bride’s mother and step-father. Mrs. Griffin
wore a deep heliotrope velvet dress, the front petticoat
of silk being covered with black wide-mesh lace
or canvas, embroidered in a large pattern. Her
white bonnet rendered her pale and delicate

face yet paler, and it was evident she had
not recovered I.oui her late i.lncs ; . A inoni i.t
later the bride came, attended by her eldest half-
sister, Miss Blanche Griffin, who is a most beautiful
o-iri of eighteen, quite as beautiful as Miss Anderson,
though lacking the sweet nobility which characterises
“ Our Mary.” The bridesmaid’s dress was reported to be
made by Worth, but it seemed to me too simple in design,
though exceedingly pretty and suiting its wearer to per-
fection. It was of soft grey cashmere, the round yoke of
white silk, the bodice of grey being drawn up on to the
silk. Round the bottom of the bodice was a thick plaited
twist of coni in white, knotted in front on tire left side,
and hanging to the bottom of the skirt. A hat of white
tulle, very simple and young-looking, and a bouquet
of pink roses with white streamers finished the
toilette. The bride, who was met at the door by
her brother, though, 1 believe her step-father gave
her away, looked very charming in a dress of
cream satin, the train perfectly plain, the front of
the petticoat slightly veiled in lace, and the bodice
covered with very fine seed pearl embroidery, which was
so fine that it almost looked like lace. She wore a plain
tulle veil, and carried a lovely bouquet. As she drove
away after the ceremony was over the people sent up two
ringing cheers. One disagreeable incident made mo in-
dignant that there should he such people in the world.
As soon as the bride and bridegroom got into their car-
riage they pulled down the blind on one side, the window
being open. A person dressed as a lady, determined not
to he defrauded of her view, rushed forward, and miming
beside the quickly-moving carriage lifted up (he blind
and looked in! How increasingly glad, after Mint
little contretemps, the wedding party must have
been that they refused to allow anyone into
the church, though the privacy observed was also
partly due to a recent death in the Navarro family.
The church was most beautifully decorated with while
flowers and tall palms, two of which formed an arch in
front of the altar. Nearly all the flowers had been
arranged by the bride herself, who was aC work at the
the church until late the night before, with her bride-
groom to assist. The wedding breakfast, the only guest
outside the small family being the priest who married
them, took place at Mrs. Griffin’s house at 16 Frognal,
Hampstead. Soon after three the happy couple departed.
The bride wore over her brown dress a dark-blue cloth
cloak made with yoke anil cape, and a big blue hat with
blue ostrich-feathers. Poor things ! They went away in
blissful igiioranoa that on the rool of the carriage a white
sal in slipper was reclining in solitary state. It is
rumoured that the happy pair are going to Venice, but as
the bride made her friends promise not to reveal her des-
tination this must be received with a grain of salt.
Dr. Luys, the celebrated Drench physician,
hypnotises (that is t<o say, mesmerises) patients in
two minutes, by causing them to look at revolving
mirrors, and so far he has had no accident. But
it is obvious that the new power is capable of ter-
rible abuse. A patient arrives full of life, in com-
plete contact with the external world : he talks
and laughs gaily; but if we only make him fix Ins
eye on a definite object, lay our fingers lightly on
the balls of his eyes, gently press the lobe of his
ear, and make him hear a slight noise, we at once
bring him to a state of utter annihilation both as
regards Ids faculties and his motive power, lie
falls on the floor in a state of coma, thunderstruck,
so to speak, and simply lies there like an inert,
flabby, senseless mass, utterly dead to the
touch of the external world. He is no
longer his own master, and is completely
at the mercy of the hypnotiser who controls him.
We then open his eyelids, we cause a flashing
light to penetrate right into his eyes; the light
passes into his brain, and proceeds to cause
special kinds of activity and to illuminate certain
special departments of the brain. A new condi-
tion is now produced, the condition of catalepsy.
This condition is marked by the pre-eminence of
optical impressions which exercise absolute sway
over all those activities of the nervous system
which are aroused. The patient’s eyes are wide
open, fixed and motionless ; the pupil is especially
affected. His excessive power of sight reaches
such an extraordinary pitch of acuteness that if
we cover his e eli Is with a layer of cotb u
wool and i..-u put a newspaper in front
of his eyes, we are amazed to see that lie
can read it —no doubt through some tiny cracks
imperceptible to us. Suppose we show to him,
behind a wooden screen one-fifth of an inch thick,
balls of coloured glass, calculated by their colours
to arouse in him different emotions; the visual
faculty is so super-perceptive that the patient feels
through the screen the different vibrations of light,
and reacts correlatively. Show him, for example,
behind this screen a blue ball, ho will exhibit signs
of sadness; show him a yellow one, and he will be
all gaiety and hilarity; and soon. And at the
same time with this extra-physiological develop-
ment of his optic nerves, we remark that the
movements of tin 1 cutaneous teguments and of
the mucous membranes are utterly paralysed.
A man in this cataleptic condition ceases to be a
man, and becomes a machine, and a machine
which, when set in motion, develops extraordinary
force. In fact, the man becomes a machine with
springs wound up to produce any kind of move-
ment, and once started, lie may be seen to repeat
a series of actions connected with his ordinary
habits. Tims, to a patient who is accustomed to
knit, without saying a word you hand the needles
and the ball of worsted, and he sets to work at
once like a genuine machine and knits without
stopping for a moment, and without the. slightest
distraction, and can go on in the same way for six
hours consecutively, even forgetting the times for
meals. After catalepsy comes somnambulism. A
patient is no longer himself, nor connected with
any of his old ties ; he is released from his past
and dissevered from his surroundings; he passively
submits to any external impulses and willingly
accepts the most utter absurdities. He will even
abdicate his own personality and clothe himself in
the borrowed personality of any person just as the
experimenter bids him. You can stick a pin with
impunity into the skin of the patient, nip him
with pincers, burn him with a hot cautcriscr, and
still he has no sensation and remains utterly un-
aware of what is going on. He continues to talk
if questions are put to him. His mucous mem-
branes are similarly deadened. When.a hypno-
tised person awakes, he is completely oblivious
of all that has happened. His consciousness
has been completely arrested. Sometimes
they are only half awake. Hr. Luys says.:—
If, indeed, the patient, only half-awakened, is
sent back in this condition to his daily life, he has
only a half-consciousness of the nature of his acts.
He may walk straight on without knowing where
he is going, or collide with persons passing by ; he
may knock them down, steal articles exposed in
shops, commit all kinds of offences, even outrages
on decency; and this unconscious person, whom
the public and the magistrates regard as in itosses-
sion of all his senses, may find himself the object
of undeserved punishment. 1 cannot, therefore,
too earnestly warn young experimenters against
the serious consequences of an incompletely-
effected awakening.
Mr. M. C. Cowlishaw has joined the
board of the Australian Newspaper Co., which is
now stated to be a paying concern. As the
Cowlishaws are a hard-headed and proverbially
lucky family, this accession to the directorate
should bring good fortune to the Star.
The total production of wool in N.S. Wales
for the year ended June 30, 1890, was 710,970
bales. The shipments from Australia and 'N.Z.
for the past year were 1,463,000 bales, and it is
thus evident that N.S.W. »alone produces quite
one half of the wool grown in Australia.
A “ Domestic Servants’ Association ” has
been started in Melbourne. Secretary Monish
declares that, “as it is desired to secure the
confidence of employers, only fully-qualified ser-
vants will be allowed to join the Association,
The principle paraded here is radically vicious.
Setting aside the fact that standards vary as to
what “ fully-qualified ” consists in, the creation of
a privileged class of domestic servants will cause
social conditions to bear still more degradingly
and cruelly on the (supposedly) imperfect out-
siders. Mr. Morrish had better style his kitten
“The Society of Perfect Treasures.” The one
broad rule to be observed should be the admission
of all domestic servants who are willing to join for
the purposes of mutual co-operation, subject to
which let each stand on her merits. If Mr.
Morrish fancies he can solve the servant-girl
question by establishing an aristocracy of slavey-
dom, he oughtn’t to be entrusted with the
destinies of a movement whose object is or should
be the disenthralment of drudges.
Ask Yourself
Ask yourself these questions : In what condition
am I to-day ? Am 1 perfectly sound in body and
in mind? Am I strong and vigorous, and is life a
constant pleasure to me ? If you can answer these
questions affirmatively you are indeed lucky. If
you are tired, weak, weary, or despondent, you
need assistance. The trying time of the year is
approaching with its dangers to physical health.
Do not neglect a headache ; it is a sign that your
liver is deranged, and may lead to permanent
disease if not attended to. If you are troubled
with restless nights, neuralgia, indigestion, ner-
vousness, or constipation, don’t treat such positive
symptoms of approaching physical decline with
contempt, or bitterly, will yon rue your carelessness.
Such invidious foes are more dangerous and fatal
than all the wars and famines that ever eventuated.

N.S.W., writes as under, date July 23rd I have
much pleasure in testifying to the efficiency of
Clements Tonic, in curing me of congestion of
the liver. For seven years I have suffered from
constant enlargement of the liver, which caused
me severe pain, and was due to a sedentary life
and want of exercise, my life being almost insup-
portable. I was listless and irritable, and at
times had to consult doctors, and was at one time
laid on a sick bed ; but all to no purpose. Suffer-
ing in this way for many years, I resorted to tin
use of Clements Tonic, and after the use of three
bottles the swelling of the liver was reduced, and
with its use pain disappeared. After taking five
bottles I can now eat and drink anything, and
smoke mypipewithout that continual irritable and
sleepy feeling resulting, I trust this letter will lead
similar sufferers to the use of Clements Tonic,
giving it a fair trial, not merely using one bottle,
and then discarding it, and I am satisfied they
will derive the same benefit. Mr. James Prentice,
proprietor of Newtown (N.S.W.) Chronicle, has
for some time been a most acute sufferer from livei
complaint, lassitude, indigestion, &c., due to busi
less care and close application to work. He took
.T.emexts Tonic. — For some time I have
suffered considerably from lassitude and in-
digestion, resulting from close attention to
work. To obtain relief I tried all sorts of so-
called “cures,” but found none that gave me any
ease. I was at last induced to use Clements
Tonic, and having done so, regret not having
taken it sooner. After taking only two bottles
my lassitude vanished. I was able to attend to
my duties with my usual energy, and also to relish
my food and enjoy my rest, and in a way that had
been unknown for some time. I shall always re-
commend Clements Tonic, believing that it only
requires to bo tried to bo appreciated.

Personal Items.
W. G. Grace weighs 17st. 81bs.
Mr. Salomons, Q. 0., goes to Europe again
next month.
Young Goschen is to be one of Governor
Jersey’s suite.
John Dillon has a private competence in-
herited from his father.
“ Tasrna ” writes a short Australian tale in
a late number of the Family Herald.
The distinguished Professor Drummond
eschews Melbourne as too religiously fanatical.
Stanley got from Sampson Low and Co.
precisely £20,000 for the copyright of his hook.
The “ Queen’s English ” is a kind of
bastard Hanoverian. It is no crime to murder it.
Although the Premier Permanents were
teetotallers, they lavished a lot of their money on
the “soakers.”
It is no longer a secret that the Empress
of Austria has heeu increasingly insane ever since
her son's suicide.

Crimean soldier. Of course he died in the Benevo-
lent Asylum.
Charles Clark is a pupil of Spurgeon's.
However, he has considerably improved on his

Girl of the Future,” in the Universal Review,

that of a stud-groom.
Lord Carington is sitting for his portrait
in oils. Why not in lardy-dah? The portrait is
to grace Government House, but then a portrait
in oils can grease anything.
The latest is to call Stanley “the Joshua
of Africa” —a good slay’-em-all name. Go up and

Archibald Forbes, in his new “ Life of
Havelock,” writes of the jaunty style in which
an English General went to fight the Mahrattas.
He took the women-folk, on elephants, to see the
savages hook it, but the first thing was a Mahratta
cannon-shot, which shifted an elephant’s ear.
American ladies, by the way, went out in their
carriages to see the rebels licked at Bull Run.
Brigand Stanley is described by an old ac-
quaintance as being now thoroughly hardened
and hopelessly indifferent to everything, except
Africa. “He does not complain of ill-health, but
the elasticity of his nature has departed. He never
laughs, and when he smiles it is apparently with
a painful effort. He is emphatically a man of one
idea to-day.” Which seems to offer a somewhat
monotonous prospect for Mrs. Brigand Stanley.

of the cases in which the senior aforesaid has held
briefs. Similar patronage was tried some years
since in Melbourne —with results which are re-
membered to this day by the offending legal mag-
nate concerned.
POLITICAL POINTS
The population of South Australia has not
increased since 1884.
The new Victorian Assembly chamber will
only hold a hundred members.
“Cromwell is not dead in England, but
only sleeps.”- Cardinal Manning.
John Burns, agitator-, has to leave the
London County Council, because he is moneyless.
John Haynes calls himself an “ M.P.”
Now M.P.’s come from Cork, not from Australia.
John Hurley, who represents Lithgow in
the N.S.W. Assembly, has, it is claimed, become
a Single-taxer.
“No compensation” to Irish landlords;
“no compensation” to English publicans, are
Radical cries in Britain just now.
Gillies’ Budget is described as the shortest
on record, but Verdon used to be shorter; he
wound up the finances in 40 minutes.
Forty three people own land valued at
£50,000 and upwards in S. A. Twenty-four land-
owners own over £lOO,OOO worth each.
The outcome of the fund for the Balaclava
000 will be that a large balance will remain when
the last man shall have died of starvation.
Herbert Burrows says that Anarchism is
Social-Democracy, so far as it is intelligible; in
its unintell gibility it is undoubtedly Anarchistic.
Commissioner Speight looks blue over the
heavy Victorian Railway deficit. Working ex-
penses, estimated at 57 per cent, reached 08 per
cent.
Now that the N.S.W. authorities have had
(heir eyes opened in the matter of illicit distilla-
tion, they are managing to find illicit stills all over
the place.
Says the Commonweal : —“ The clerical
brand of Socialism is a very-spread species of
wooden ham with which the worker’s stomach is
to be filled.” This is a memo for Rev. Be van.
Gillies has worked off his fifth Budget
consecutively, thus breaking the record of Vic-
torian Treasurers. People thought he would never
see a second, but he is a better Premier than
Service.
The new Melbourne Cathedral peal of
bells burst up an Unemployed meeting on an
adjacent patch the other night. At one time the
Church used to be the friend of the poor and
needy, and him that hath no helper.
A Melbourne hatter, with a wife and nine
children, shot himself dead last week, as a mild
protest against the assumption of Gillies and
Patterson that there are no unemployed. Carlyle
tells us of the poor woman who proved her rela-
tionship to the rich by giving them the cholera.
In the House of Commons a number of
members are waiting with 1016 amendments on
the Irish Land Purchase Bill. They want to
amend everything except the “Whereas” at the
beginning of the measure, and somebody is lying
low to further amend pretty nearly every one of
these 1016 amendments.
The £9500-worth of Italian statuary, &c.,
bought on Government account from Mr. Oscar
Meyer by Sir Henry Parkes is not all that
N.S.W. has purchased in that line during the
last few years. What has become of the statuary
or statuettes said to have been traded off to some-
one for N.S.W. Treasury money by the ingenious
Mr. Hastings Malcolm ?
The attempt to make people nominate
their religion, in the English Census, has failed.
The Bishops & Co. tried it in the Lords, but when
it failed the Archbishop of Canterbury blandly
disavowed that it was official. Yet the whole
was a paltry trick to get the unbelievers or in-
diffevents registered in the all-embracing Church
of England, and prove that Disestablishment
wasn’t wanted.
Sydney University, as at present consti-
tuted, is, it becomes increasingly plain, a Tory in-
stitution, the chief purpose whereof is to provide
Englishmen with fat billets. For a brainy Aus-
tralian to hope for promotion on its teaching staff
is madness —the utmost that can be hoped for by
an Austrahan-born scholar is a miserable lecture-
ship of £3OO or £4OO a year. Parliament should
deal with this matter with a seven-inch knife.
Crick, M.L.A., was perfectly right in
demanding a select committee to inquire into the
working of the N.S.W. Registrar-General’s
department. Thousands of pounds have lately
been stolen from that institution, and the public
have a perfect right to know all about the steal-
ing. Treasurer M‘Millan’s plea that some
alterations have taken place in the office is not
good enough. The place has for years been a den
of thieves, and ought to be publicly purged.
Ernest Twopeny, of journalistic and Ex-
hibition fame, has issued in New Zealand an
election-address which does credit to his ability as
a newspaper and show man. He caters for all.
He sweeps up Radicals of every hue, Protec-
tionists, Unionists, Socialists, Nationalism's of
land, bursters-up of the same, Catholics, Bible-in-
schools people, and the unemployed. Outside of
Twopeny’s net, says a fellow-scribe, there is
nothing left but banks, loan-companies, and
journalists.
The Inniskilling Royal Fusiliers broke out
into melody recently, and sang “ God save.*
Ireland, ’ and now they are charged with “ in-
subordination ” in consequence. Any Irishman
who is found offering a suggestion that God should
save anything except the Queen and Balfour and
Batten berg and the Tory majority in the Commons
is liable to get into difficulties in the fine and
enlightened British empire. Even if he wants to
be saved himself he is looked upon with dark
suspicion, it being an insurrectionary act for any
one to look after h-’s own soul so long as the
Government has got one to be attended to.
We regret to learn that the N.S.W. Rail'
way Commissioner will in future “ refuse to recog'
nise” the Railway and Tramway Employes
Association. No doubt it will continue to be
there just the same, but Mr. Eddy announces that
he will be ignorant of the fact, and if his eye
happens to light upon the Association, which is a
pretty large object and not easily overlooked, he
will fail to see anything, and will assume a hollow,
weary glare to intimate that he is looking at
vacancy. What we desire to know now is whether
the Association will decline to recognise Mr.
Eddy, or whether it will take cognisance of that
gentleman and step-upon him in order to draw his
attention. Also, we would mention to the Com-
missioner an instance of a man who declined to
recognise an elephant, and thereby proved the
uselessness of officially ignoring an object larger
than himself, for the elephant squashed him just
the same.
Nervous Exhaustion.
FROM THE AMERICAN RURAL HOME.
The too rapid exhaustion of nervous vitality is
the great American disease that is carrying off
thousands and tens of thousands of our race, says ■
the San Francisco News Letter. Why should /
we be more amenable to this trouble than other
people? We never hear of English or German
nervous exhaustion; yet the difficulty is one of
every day occurrence in this country, and all
around us there are victims of it. The tendency
in Europe is toward an increase of years ; men are
now living to healthier and more advanced ages
than ever before. All the great characters who
have in recent years occupied Jthe public stage,
may be taken to illustrate what we mean. Moltke,
Gladstone, and Bismarck are conspicuous examples
of vigorous minds in healthy bodies at a period of
life seldom attained in this country. The obituary
column of the London Times shows that it is the
commonest thing possible for Englishmen to live
to be eighty or ninety years old. The death
notices in our own papers seldom afford such
evidences of age. Why should there be this differ-
ence?
If a long life and a moderate enjoyment of it
were sought as the chief ends to be realised by
American men and women, there is no reason why
they should not go on reasonably and comfortably
to glorious old age. Some do. But with most of
us, we eat in too great a hurry, dine in crowds,
think “ as quick as a flash,” bother ourselves not at
all about digestion, which for that very reason is
nearly always bothering us, sleep only when we
must, and awake to renewed tumults of greed and
ambition that fret the flesh off our bones, use up
our vital force, and finally extinguish the vital
spark. That is what is the matter with the
Americans. It is hardly possible, we suppose, to
expect the restless; ambitious Americans of this
generation to practice the “Gospel of rest” ad-
vocated by Herbert Spencer. If they would,
however, but realise the fact, as many have al-
ready done, that by keeping the main purifying
organs of the system, the kidneys and liver, in
healthy working order by the use of Warner’s safe
cure there would be very little danger of a general
break down (the usual result of nervous ex-
haustion), and more people would live to green
old age with unimpaired energies.
Note. —The exigencies of life in these Austra-
lian colonies are daily becoming more akin to the
experiences expressed in the above recital of
American fast-living, and its evil influences on
the general health of the people. Therefore, the
moral to be gained therefrom cannot be too
forcibly impressed upon public attention.

for all her girlhood, were purchased, together with

about £llOO.

THE POWER OF WOMAN’S TONGUE.
“Mr. and Mrs. Esther are new in the Debtors’ Prison at Darlinghurst. Mrs. Esther slandered a neighbour, who sued for damages and made Mr. Eather defenant in the action, the costs and
damages in which being unsatisfied, the two defendants were torn from their family and incarcerated under the barbarous New South Wales slander-law.-Daily Paper.
WIFE: “ALAS’! TO THINK THAT MY TONGUE SHOULD HAVE GOT US IN HEBE, AND CAST OUR SEVEN CHILDREN ON THE CHARITY OF A
COLD WORLD. WOULD THAT IT COULD GET US OUT AS EASILY!” THOSE
HUSBAND (in the security of his cell): “WELL, MY DEAR, YOUR TONGUE IS A PERFECT RASPER. SUPPOSE YOU TRY IT ON ONE OF THOSE
IKON BARS?”

Society
thus, the mineral is made vegetable, the vegetable
animal, the animal man, and man God, but the
different stages of this evolution do not take
place on the same planet. The Hindoos say that
on our planet it is impossible that a monkey be-
come man, although his evolution takes place in
the invisible world. The mineral becomes god,
but god also becomes mineral. This change, by
the Hindoos, is called involution.
# * #
Progressive materialisation of Divine
force explains the mystery of the Incarnation,
taught by all religions, and the progressive deifica-
tion of matter explains the mystery of the Re-
demption.
# # *
In Buddhists’ opinion, the fundamental
mysteries of Christianity are translations ©f the
ancient Hindoo religion. The Buddhist’s cate-
chism gives long details on the life of Buddha, a
life, that aside from the miracles, is similar to that
of Jesus. Morally and intellectually, Buddha
surpassed all men, and his name means illuminated
or perfect wisdom. Buddha fasted in the desert,
but he soon discovered that only by meditation can
one learn truth and wisdom. His intelligence, like
the lotus flower, bloomed into beauty, and the
four great truths were then made clear.
These truths are, first: all existence necessarily
involves pain and suffering ; second, all suffering
is caused by craving for wealth, for sensual
pleasure and for existence j third, cessation of
suffering is simultaneous with extinction of this
craving; fourth, extinction of this craving is ac-
complished by perseverance in the noble eight-fold
path—correct belief, correct thought, correct
speech, correct doctrine, correct means of exis-
tence, correct effort, correct memory, and correct
meditation.
# * *
The man who walks in this path reaches
‘Nirvana,’ the condition where change ceases,
If the authority of the Sydney Evening
News oablegrammer (who is solid in Court circles)
is to be depended on, the coining Governor of New
South Wales has already “sown his wild oats,”
having, during his Oxford career, given post-obits
covering the whole family property, which were,
however, nullified later on on appeal to the law,
and it would be absurd, therefore, to hint that he
conies out here to avoid a reaping of the inevitable
harvest. His wife, Margaret Elizabeth, eldest
daughter of the second Lord Leigh, born in 1849,
who has made her already affluent lord wealthy
with six pledges oi wifely affection and is currently
believed to be the Governor’s governor, is evidently
a woman of some intelligence, or at least of suffi-
cient mental independence to follow the lead
Of those who dare to think for themselves,
for she is said to be a Buddhist. Buddhism, the
story of whose founder, Gautama, and his Renun-
ciation is so beautifully told in Edwin Arnold’s
“Light of Asia,” has been for some time highly
popular in British aristocratic circles; and if
rumour has spoken truly concerning Lady Jersey s
belief in it, Buddhism, which may be briefly de-
scribed as the religion of eternal repose, is likely
also to become popular in Sydney, where Lord
Oarington’s cathedral yawn, and the general
drowsiness of all worshippers in all temples from
St. James’s to St. Joss’s, have to some extent
paved the way for its reception.

But, to return to our Jersey muttons. My
lord has, as we have already remarked, a shock
head of red hair, is currently asserted to be
generously - hoofed, and is altogether such a
person that his appearance at the tail-end of
any procession would not call forth any special
remarks. Possibly it is this homeliness that
has given biith to the cablegram - conveyed
fear that he will not prove a success as a
Governor, and if that official be accounted
merely an official M.C. or a social adornment in
the name of the Queen, then there may be some
grounds for this jeremiad. On the whole, how-
ever, we think that Jersey, having heaps of money
to spend, will —if he only spends it -be popular,
for beau tv is largely a thing of custom. Ideas of
beauty differ with race and country, and his
arrival will probably result in a complete reversion
of our standard of masculine good looks and hoist
the man with large feet, fiery locks, and a
thwarted-looking, ginger moustache on the very
apex of comeliness alongside the Apollo Belvidere.
The Jerseys sail for New South Wales on October
24, and “ My Lady’s ” brother, Captain Rupert
Leigh, of the 15th Hussars, comes along as aide-
de-camp—an item of news that is calculated to
make every “society” feminine hearty whether
virgin or otherwise, throb with ex-
pectation akin to torture.
# * *
Five hundred million people on this
globe are Buddhists, and in France alone there
are thirty thousand Buddhists. It is not gene-
rally known that Richard Wagner was a fervent
Buddhist. But the European thinkers do nqt'
practise the doctrines of Buddhism; that is why,a
certain young Viennese student has caused such
consternation in the church. He is ascetic, fasts,
or eats only fruits and vegetables, envelops him-
self in a hempen bag, and already treats with in-
difference physical suffering. Continuing in this
manner, he would soon be the incarnation of
Buddha, and the authorities talk of expulsion
from the University, for fear of his influence on
the other students. The practice of Buddhism is
said to cultivate a sixth sense, that in non-believers
is always latent. By this sense, one can foretell
good or bad fortune, and the meditation that calls
this sense is always physical, psychological, and
physiological. Physically, the body must obey
the mind; psychologically, all human organic
forces must be concentrated on the development
of the sixth sense—intuition ; and physiologically,
respiration must be regulated to control the
expenditure of vital force. Soon as this sixth sense
is acquired, the Buddhists are capable of knowing
nature’s secrets, and of producing phenomena tha,t
seem supernatural, but are in reality the mani-
festation of forces, very natural, but not yet
understood. Buddhism is a religion created for
the Aryan race, while Catholicism is of Semitic
origin—for this reason, the Buddhistic movement
is a fitting accompaniment to the Anti-Semitic
warfare.
# * *
Buddhism respects everything that has
life, and Buddhists believe in re-incarnation, not
in metempsvchosis; Christian missionaries have
tried to create confusion in order to prejudice
against Buddhism. The Buddhists believe that if
a rich man sin, after death he enjoys a certain
repose to repay him for the sufferings in this life,
but his punishment begins at the moment he is
obliged to again return on this or another planet.
Then his spirit enters the body of some miserable
person, and the more guilty he has been in the
frevious existence, the less chance he has in this,
n the same way a person who has led a righteous
life in a previous existence is in this re-incarnated
in the body of a rich person. There is the origin
of evil and suffering. Fifteen hundred years must
separate two re-incarnations, and the infernal
regions do not exist, as real suffering takes place
on earth. No omnipotent God interposes after
death, as, according to the law of fatality, man is
his own judge. This is called the law of Karma.
Hindoos believe in evolution, but their ideas of
evolution are very different from those of Euro-
peans ; for them, evolution stops neither with man
nor the present life.
# * *
Each human being is developed, first
physically, then intellectually, then morally,
until he becomes a Buddha, that is, a divine
being, who, after death, becomes God. The re-
incarnations continue until one becomes Buddha ;
THE REASON WHY.
JONES (to Smith): “SHOCKING THING THIS BANK FRAUD IN WHICH
OUR FRIEND JACK IS IMPLICATED. POOR DEVIL, WONDER
WHAT MADE HIM CLEAR OUT SO QUICK?”
BARMAID (promptly) : “’CAUSE HE HAD THE MONEY TO DO IT WITH.”
where there is no sorrow, no suffering, where all
that relates to physical man is forgotten, obli-
terated, and where repose is perfect. As already
stated, one attains * Nirvana ’ only after having
passed through successive existences, more or less
numerous, according to the degree of wisdom
exhibited in each. Such is the Buddhist creed.
* * *
A cable message in the Melbourne Argus
states that Lady Jersey was born at Stoneleigh
Park, adjacent to the village of Stoneleigh, where
Sir Henry Parkes had his origin. We presume
this item was sent out as a warning to Parkes, so
that he might have time to gather up his infant
and his injured leg, and his unfinished poem about
Stella, and to grasp the family plate, and the
crest, and the works of those great minds that he
loves to commune with in his leisure moments,
and fly secretly in disguise by the back door before
Jersey arrives to take vengeance on him for being
born in the same parish as his wife. And if this
wasn’t the object of the message, then we would
like to know what in thunder the object of the
message was anyhow. For there doesn t seem to
be much in the cablegram as an item of news, and
even if the Argus had gone on to state that the
new Governor had once fallen down heavily in the
same public-house as Sir Samuel Griffith nobody
would have been thrilled by the intelligence.
# * *
Governor Carington considers that Gover-
nor Philip’s statue should be cast in shining brass
instead of in dull bronze. This being so, why
shouldn’t the Queen Victoria statue in King-
street, Sydney, be re-cast in glittering brass?
With what pleasure should we watch the opera-
tion of washing the old lady every morning in
oxalic acid, like a doctor’s sign !
There is a whiff of glory in a fine lady
being driven out of the Government House gates—
we mean in a carriage—and being mistaken for
Lady Carington or Lady Hopetoun. In England
the idea used to be to get mistaken for the
Princess of Wales, but now it is more agreeable to
be regarded as the Duchess of Fife. The same
woman does it —or thinks so. What’s the odds,
so long as you’re happy ?
* * *
A Wellington (N.Z.) zephyr joyfully played
old Harry the other day in a job-printing office.
The printer’s devil was carrying an armful of
pawnbroker’s tickets from the cutting-room, when
the breeze lifted a score of them and deposited
them in the gutter a couple of blocks away. One
Peggs, a funny man, saw them, took them home,
and in a fit of inspiration filled them all in with
prominent names, and then carefully dropped
them one by one in likely places—one in the bar of
a swell barmaid, another in a tram-car, one or
two in a big club, some in church, and others at a
jam-tart “ small and early.” And now the upper-
crust has any amount of small scandal to amuse
afternoon-tea’ers with, and the De Joneses firmly
believe that the De Drowns are so low down as to
have to pawn their silver spoons.

who would like her “ subjects ” to style her “ Your
Excellency,” is about to issue a cookery-book,

as wild fowl, chicken or duck.

Pitt, Son and Badgery, of Sydney, lately
sent samples of their frozen meat to Lord Mayor
Isaacs, of London. The Lord Mayor, in a
courteous reply, says that he and his friends
“found it excellent.” As we presume,that Pitt,
Son and Badgery’s mutton is not kosher, we are
afraid that Mr, Isaacs will shortly find himself in
trouble with the Rabbinical authorities.

An ex-patient complains that they boil the
eggs very hard at Cooktown Hospital. The
present writer was once breakfasting at Parra-
matta with a certain well-known seventy year-old
doctor, whose complexion is perfectly beautiful
and whose attention to his digestive apparatus
is almost a matter of notoriety. He ordered eggs
• —“hard, boiled seven minutes full,” and explained
that the longer eggs were boiled the more easily
they were assimilated, “ provided you chewed ’em
properly.” Now, what are you to believe?

# # *
Some years ago a Murrumburrah (N.S.W )
Chinaman bought a black horse out of the local
pound-yard. He put him in his paddock, and in
the night the larrikins painted him white. Seeing
a white horse in his paddock the next day, lie
turned him out, and commenced a search for his
horse. The white horse, poor animal, died from
being painted, but the Chinaman’s black horse has
never since been seen. The Bulletin i (‘com-
mends this idea to theatrical managers. The
Chinaman, however, should be made equal to the
occasion, and should, on finding his black horse
exchanged for a white one, set to work to blacken
the latter. We haven t any time to write fare s
ourselves.

with aspirations above h : s station—well, the

such as ex-Queen Isabella and charmers of her
description are bound to receive.

* * * ,
Ernest Hutchinson, the boy-pianist, sent
“home” to complete his musical education, is
evidently working out his destiny. Pianny-play-
ing must run in the family, for a little brother and
sister give fair promise of beating Ernest in due
course. Papa Hutchinson is a blacksmith (a
musical blacksmith, apparently) down at Laverton,
one of those melancholy land-boom townships,
situated some 16 miles out of Melbourne, in which
it would seem impossible for any child to cultivate
a soul.
# # *
The coming organ for Melbourne Cathedral
hears the inscription—“ To the glory of God and
in thanksgiving for many mercies, this organ was
given, &c., by Thomas Dyer Edwardes, London.”
The many mercies referred to were abnormal r ises
in the price of Melbourne land bequeathed to the
grateful Edwardes by his late papa. The organ is,
in fact, a kind of thanksgiving for the land-boom,
which enriched Mr. E. to such an extent that he,
staggered by the exuberance of his unearned in-
crement, suggested that he ought to do something
for the Australians. Yes, really ! Whereupon
some influential party proposed a peal of bells and
organ for the Cathedral. The bells came out a
year ago, and now the organ is riding the briny
-wave per sailing vessel —an economical arrange-
ment that will delay the opening of the big taber-
nacle until close upon Christmas. The original
idea was to have everything in working order by
“ Cup” week, so that the unrepentant bookmaker
might get a national opportunity to save his souk
# # *
It is alleged that somebody has mastered
the art of making cloth from broken glass. Should
this discovery develope practical results we shall
have the small boys breaking windows to provide
their mothers with the necessary material for
patching their little pants.
* * *
The prevalence of suicide in Victoria lias
inspired a Mr. Montague Solomons to write to the
Age asking why human beings can be so wicked
as to destroy their lives, when dumb animals are
content to live on. In reply to which another man
points out that many caged animals and birds
commit suicide by refusing food. The Bulletin
would further call attend n to the vast numbers
of rodents that wilfully take “Rough on Rats,”
instead of patiently bearing the ills they have, and
a bushman who happens to be by, tells us that he
once saw a fat poley bullock suicide by holding his
head under water in the Edwards river, near
Deniliquin.

Who still survives of those who knew the
City of the Cabbage Garden in the Fifties will
doubtless remember the Royal Hotel up in Vic-
toria-street, kept by D- : , “Bully D with
the pretty daughters,” where the teams usually
halted and all the teamsters Hung the dollars about
in galore—(carriage to Beech worth was £lB5 per
ton in those balmy days) —intoxicating themselves
equally with the smiles bestowed on them with
careful na'ivete by the charming Soylla-and-Charyb-
dis-like syrens as with the snake-juice shaken from
the shilling-a-nobbler bottle. Peeping into such a
vista of the Fifties we see many of the faces we
have since known so well, and amongst them the
braw Alick Robertson, of afterday Cobb and Co.
fame, much booted, bearded, spurred, red-jumpered,
and oabbage-tree-hatted, cheek-by-jowl with Geo.
and John Whittingham, all calculating, no doubt,
their chances of getting the teams down again
before the rain sets in. Passing on to the Sixties,
we find the Royal bereft of beauty, and Alick,
John, and George made and mated in triple alli-
ance. Again we pass on to 1890. Scene : London
by gaslight, within Lord Aberdeen’s mansion
where the gay and blue-blooded throng elbows its
way into the royalty-honoured presence of the
host. The Duke of Park-lane and the Duchess
of Pimlico re-echo the praises of so fine a fellow as
our host, dontcherkuow. Our host is Alick
Robertson.
* * *
A certain Victorian farmer, tired of work,
went to bed for seven years pretending to be sick.
He refused medical aid, and accounted for his eat-
ing-power by telling his wife be had galloping
consumption. He might have been in bed to this
day had not the family gone out one night to a
dance, leaving him in charge of a man who went
to sleep. The consumptive got up and had a good
look round at the old place—the place he had not
seen for so many years—and ere his return to bed
Ids absence was discovered by the man on the
watch.

Two brothers on llendinine station
(N.S.W.) had an argument about smoking. The
non-smoker said that if his brother saved his
money instead of buying tobacco with it lie would
soon have a tidy sum put by; and as proof lie
agreed to save every week the amount his brother
spent on tobacco. In a short time he had saved
£3, with which he bought a nice heifer, and great
was his triumph over Ids wicked smoking brother.
The progeny of the heifer did not in course of
time amount to £lO9, with which he selected a
piece of land and made a fortune. In a week,
alas, the heifer died. Which tale reminds us of the
dying pauper Frenehman : “I have never smoked
in all my life, but where, O where is the fortune 1
have saved in cigars ?” There may be more nourish-
ment in cow-juice than in cigars, but there is more
fnu in cigars than in cow-jnice.

# * *
Australia is now being largely circularised
for funds in aid of the London mission of a Mr.
F. N. Charrington (obviously a keen advertiser),
who claims to have voluntarily renounced a
quarter of a- million’s worth of shares in certain
London breweries because of his detestation of
the drink-traffic. It is as well to note that Mr,
N. Edward Charrington writes to the London
Press declaring that Mr. F. N. Charrington afore-
said never was a partner, and never owned a
shilling in either of the Charrington breweries ;
and also that the latter’s entire fortune, which ho
received from his father at die latter’s death, was
made in connection with the brewery business.
# * *
The associated doctors who are disputing
with certain Sydney suburban friendly society
lodges as to the price of medical attendance point
out that they are only asking £1 per annum
for each member—-including attendance on the
member, his wife and family, which may include
any fraction of a dozen children —while at present
the club doctors are getting but 2|d per week per
member; that in London the dispensaries get an
average of fid per week per member, and here,
where wages are much higher, the doctors’
demand must be regarded as extremely moderate.
They complain that the friendly society men,
although trades-unionists, endeavour to beat them
down to the last cent., and are offended at their
resolution to maintain decent rates of remunera-
tion for work which at times is most arduous. It
is stated that out of the 800 doctors in N.S.W.,
over GOO have already notified their intention to
support the Western Suburbs Medical Association.
The Bulletin thinks that the friendly societies
are in this matter over-reaching themselves.
Heaven help the patient who selects' his doctor on
the basis of the lowest tender !

# * *
An energetic Australian financial agent
has a standing advertisement in the pap -vs, in
viting two. men to call at his office and hear o
legacies which have been left them in England.
'Whether the two men ever do call seems doubtful,
but at any rate the ad. keeps the advertiser's name
before the public. He doesn't keep enquiring for
the same two men all the time ; after about a
fortnight he drops them and advertises for two
more. From this it would appear that he never
finds either of them, or else, that he finds both of
them simultaneously just in time to take their
names out and advertise for the other two, who
also have got a legacy left them in England-
anyhow, he never drops one and keeps on calling
for the other. The one reason, however, which
makes ns think lie doesn’t find them is that it
seems almost impossible to find any man, let alone
a long series of men, with names like those he
calls for. A financier who rises in cold blood and
invites Augustus Clamjenkins to wait upon him
is a person who is reckless of consequences, and it
is sheer waste of money to advertise incessantly
for Thomas Henry Jobkinsops, for there isn’t any
such person on the face of the earth, and never
will be—mot even if a dozen legacies were left him.

The Bishop of Ballarat has offered an insult
to the Y.M.G. A. which nothing but gore will ever
wipe out. He states that lie wants to have no
connection with that body “if its effects were
simply to create young females of the male sex, and
though he liked to see the youths put off, as the
Scripture said, the old man, he did not wish them
to put on the old woman.” The boneless young
person with the heavenly smile and the inch of
white sock showing between his pants and his
shoes is so mad at this that he has risen with his
umbrella in one hand and, his ■golosh in the other,
and has placed his milk and his bath-bun on the
table, in front of him, and lias asked in a small
voice fur an explanation. And if he doesn’t get it
the .chances are that he will let Ids carnal passions
loose and inform his mother about it.

Melbourne City Club Hotel, familiarly
known as “Lane’s, ’ which was written up and
drawn out in Tun Bulletin not very long ago, has
silently and without fireworks or any other emo-
tional display “changed hands.” The high
priestess and Chloe were one day missed from
their accustomed posts between the anchovy sand-
wiches and the coffee urn, and Tottie faded away
a few days after at ,11.80 p.ui., never to return.
There is now less musical and dramatic criticism
from behind the counter than of yore, and Chloe’s
disquisitions on Dickens, always finishing up with
“ Whisky, did yon say?" are but a sweet memory.

A Ballarat (Vic.) lady, who married a
Chinaman some years ago, now wants a divorce.
The moral of this inc’dont is considerably'damaged
by the fact that the Chinaman is bruised all over,
and wants a di force a great deal worse than she
does.
* # «
The respectable but tiring tow n of Geelong,
has been calmly simmering in the juice of
its own sanctity these last 80 or 40 years, content to
lose population, reputation, and every other ation,
so long as it could make a little bit of money and
save its little bit of souk Geelong, the happy
hunting ground of the large, fat parson, and the
head-quarters of the tea-and-muffin industry, is
making a move in the direction of sin and corn-
petition. The old family cow doesn’t wander at
large as freely as she did, for the march of pro-
gress is shoving her off the footpath and driving
her back into the wilderness with new villa resi-
dences. The croak of the bull frog is no longer
heard in the main streets, nor does the goat in-
vade the police-station with his old confidence.
They have a market-day now, and a new post-
office, and a fresh pump, and altogether Geelong is
becoming comparatively godless and go-ahead.

How much virtue rests in a barren title,
especially to a Melbourne society lady ! There is
a young gentleman now in that city who is haying
a fairly good time of it, having had the wit to
have his cards printed “Lord H , en
route,” surmounted by a coronet. A section of
the local aristocracy is apparently unaware that
there is no.such title in existence.

denial of receiving a large quantity. Defendant’s
■ lawyer: “No man could cat such a quantity in
the time, not even an Irishman !” Plaintiff’s lawyer
(Irish) ; “Oh yes he could, if lie kept pigs.”
Great laughter.

Credit where credit is due. The Chinese
introduced tobacco-growing intoTumut (N S.W.)

An American paper asks, “ Should pastors
smoke?” Yes, most of them will smoke in the
next world.
The late rise in the value of silver means
an extra £250,000 per annum added to the profits
of the Broken Hill Proprietary mine.
A Gundagai (N.S.W.) man told his wife to
pick some plums, at which she said it was too hot.
Then he out the trees down and, bringing them
inside, asked her to pull them in the shade.
An engine-driver steamed into Goulburn
station the other day with the steam rushing from
the engine in style. “ Where, in thunder, is the
train?” said the station-master. “The train!
Why, dash me, if I didn’t leave it behind !”
Dear Bulletin,- Apropos of a current dis-
cussion. A little time ago a lad Was tried in Yass
for an unnatural offence, and, in answer to a
juror’s question, he said he had learned the prac-
tice on board the Vernon.— A Countryman.
The Melbourne Jews have established an
Aid Society, which succours the distressed Lazarus
by lending him money, without charging interest.
The idea seems to be that the distressed one will
“place his loan in it a Christian” at shixty per
shent, payable in advance.
Some classes of cotton goods arc impreg*
nated with arsenic. It is customary in the trad®
to guarantee the absence of this hurtful stimulant)
and one British firm —on their registered trade-mark
beneath the inscription, “Warranted free from
arsenic*” —have a portrait of Mrs. Maybrick.
“Even when inflicting the lash,” says a
Geelong paper, “ we do not profess to do so with
a wish to indulge revenge, but with a benignant
and charitable desire to reform the offender.”
There are only two places Where the preaching of
such ghastly cant would be possible. One is Gee-
long and the other is - Hell.
Says Melbourne Evening Standard, in
condemning the system under which convicts are
placed in “irons”: —“But the spirit of the old
convict system hangs around Australian criminal
jurisprudence, and will only be got rid of by a
special and vigorous effort.” “ Yet this,” remarks
the Hamilton Spectator, “is the paper that
recently elided a half-column of frenzied shriek
for human blood with the words, ‘ Bring out the
Cat! bring out the cat!’ ”
Every Australian city has its underground
philanthropists, nervous about getting into the
papers, not liking their right hand to know, &c.
Such is Miss Sutherland, the Melbourne Miss
Rye, sending so many poor ragged boys and girls
to situations in the bush. Then little Foster
carries on his noble kindred work amid the boys,
his Worthy rival being Groom, a journeyman
hatter when he founded the Excelsior classes.
Groom’s Brighton Home for destitute boys is
beyond praise. Grand old octogenarian Dr. Sin-
gleton, wortlx a five-bushel bag of the Souls of
ordinary parsons, is still extant, though one fears
sometimes he puts rather a premium on poverty.
Melbourne ’Erald knocks all the weird
music out of Poe’s immortal line by misquoting
it—
And,that sad, uncertain rustling of each silken purple
curtain.
The hard, practical mind of the journalist natu-
rally uses the term “silken” to describe goods
hanging in front of the window, and the wonder
is that he didn’t write “purple silken curtain.”
But the dreamy bard, associating the mere ma-
terial with its ghostly rustle, wrote
And the silken, Sad, uncertain rustling of each purple
curtain.
In the shifting of one little, word is revealed the
awful difference between a dead poet and a live
reporter for an evening newspaper.
If your liver troubles you take Wolfe’s Schnapps.
A society of learned men is being formed
in England to preserve the Pyramids and the
Sphinx from final ruin and annihilation. For
4000 years or more those gigantic monuments of
the past looked down through the Egyptian air on
the races that howled and warred and clamoured
all around, and no pagan Vandal did them any
damage. “ But now the cultured Christian idiot
has started to carry away the pyramids piece-
meal, and to bang the ears and nose and other
features off the ancient statues with hammers, in
order that he may go home and put a broken
boulder on his mantelpiece, and label it, “Chunk
busted from the tomb of a dead philosopher,” or
“ Ear chopped off a venerable work of art.” The
British tourist would wrench the teeth out of a
live apostle sooner than not have a relic to take
with him, and his brother the American traveller
would paint a soap-advertisement on the part ot
the holy man that the other fellow had le't, and
would ask him for his autograph as well, and
finish up by selling him a wooden ham.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors.
Strictly Original matter contributed to Tim Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under
1, Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must he made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-boa.nl.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrit ies, or people
who ought to he celebrated.
5. HumoroUs poems (from eight to forty-eight lines in-
ferred ).
/,. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
6. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legiulv, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

Total receipts of the Victoria Racing Club for
the financial year ending June 30th were about £82,000,
the profits on last “Cup” meeting alone being close on
£lB 000. The Autumn (Champion) fixture, which seldom
or never repays the plucky outlay, showed hut a scarcely
perceptible loss of £OB this year, and altogether the Club
seems to be going ahead faster than ever.

# * #
From all accounts, Peter Jackson nearly made
a mistake with one Edward Smith, of Denver, Col.,
whom he undertook to knock out in five rounds. It
appears that upon his return to America, Jackson was not
the abstemious Jackson he ought to have been, and faced
Smith a lot above his weight and in anything but good
trim. He smashed Smith about for three rounds, Avhen
he fell weak, and it Avas more good luck than anything
else that prevented Smith from finishing him off. Peter
recovered for the fourth round, but was unable to knock
his man out in the five rounds.
# * *
Bob Fitzsimmons, the Australian boxer, who is
expected to whip Jack Dempsey, is the nearest approach
conceivable to a living skeleton. His cheek-bones,
shoulder-blades, and ribs are as much in evidence as those
in a museum, and if he should fall upon Dempsey with
one of his shin-bones he will assuredly cut Jack in halves.
KHz’S framework is covered with a network of muscle and
sinew, without an ounce of flesh, as may he judged from
the fact that, though standing six feet in height and big-
boned at that, he is amongst the middle-weights.
* * *
At “ Sam ” Matthews’ hall on Thursday night,
young Sandy. Ross beat “Joe” Meredith after boxing ten
quick rounds. Young Ross’s dehut on metropolitan boards
naturally brings to mind the historic battles between
Sandy Ross pi-re and Larry Foley. They met first at
Hunter’s Reach, Middle Harbour. The fight was a real
rough-and-tumble, and tremendous punishment was meted
out on both sides. Next time they met at George’s River,
just near the site of the present Como Bridge, but Larry
had in the meantime developed some of the science which
he afterwards showed, and he polished Ross off. The
men’s respective admirers set them up in business —putting
Larry in a pub. and Sandy in a cab. Everyone now knows
Larry, and Sandy still drives his cab.
* * *
The Australians, after having had all the best of
the match with the Lyric Club, at Barnes, were defeated
by lOd runs. Their score for 25 matches is now 10 defeats,
0 victories, (i draws. Says a London writer, after sighing
for the dashing play of Charlie Bannermann, Massie,
M'Donnell, Palmer and Giffen; “The other day, in the
match between the Australians and a M.C.C. side, the
fonder, during an hour and a quarter, scored some 32
runs, or thereabouts. Well, this was awfully slow busi-
ness, and hardly what the lookers-on ‘ went out for to
see.’ As I watched the diplomatic tactics of Messrs.
Murdoch, Trott, and their colleagues, who between them
brought about this scoring result, 1 wondered to myself
how many runs such men as our Maurice Read and Gunn
would have made against the same bowling.”

• « *
Certain enthusiasts have subscribed over £IOOO
for the purpose of finding an English sculler good enough
to bring the championship to the Thames. So far their
efforts have resulted, seemingly, in putting money in
East’s pocket, a man whom Buhear can donkey-lick, and
Buhear lias literally no show with Stanhury, Kemp,
M'Lean, Brown, and, perhaps, Matterson. To produce
world-beaters you want good material; England just now
apparently hasn’t got it, and all the money prizes in the
world can’t produce what doesn’t exist. Your chubby,
“shoulder-tied,” short-armed, heavy-limbed, beer-dulled,
“ dashless ” Britisher, who was probably only half-fed
while his l)ones were growing, has no chance with the
long-armed, lithe, brisk, elastic-limbed, dashing Australian
who does easily what the other effects only with effort. If
the Britishers give £IOOO yearly for the next 100 years
they may breed a champion; meantime, the struggle is
between America and Australia, with the latter for choice.
* * *
C. J. Thornton, “the lion-hitter,” once scored
20 in a four-ball over, playing for his University against
the Gentlemen. At Brighton lie hit a hall ICS yards from
hat to pitch, and at the Orleans Club ground, off Harry
Boyle, he lifted one 152 yards before it touched the turf.
Playing at the Oval, in a match Gentlemen of the South
v. Players of the South, in the first innings he hit up 31
in 16 minutes, and in the second innings 61 in 47 minutes.
At Scarborough he made 197 notout for the Gentlemen v.
Zingari, made up chiefly by 8 sixes and 12 fourers.

The Players of England were, probably, never
so strong in the history of cricket as to-day. Once the
professional howler was scarcely, expected to he a good
hat; now some of the best bats are among the howlers.
Good judges declare that in Players of England v. the
World, the odds would be on the former team.

William Teece, M.L.A., is dead. He re-
presented Goulburn in N.S.W. Assembly for
many years, and had a share of the brains of a
really clever family, the best-known member of
which is now Mr. Richard Teece, secretary of the
Australian Mutual Provident Society. The de-
ceased gentleman’s abilities had chiefly been
directed to the study of parliamentary precedent
and to the management of members of Parliament.
Mr. Teece was always Parkes’ whip, and Parkes
now wants somebody to lead donkeys for him.
THE CABMAN WHO HAD TO BUY
HIS WIFE A NEW FROCK.
From the WOLVERHAMPTON CHROMICLE.
We have but just learned of the thrilling experi-
ence of the well-known cabman, Charles Green,
who for many years has been employed by the
Wilson Cab Company. His experience, as re-
lated below, is as interesting as it is extraordinary.
It appears that Mr. Green had occasion recently
to go out of town, remaining overnight, awl was
obliged to sleep in a damp bed, and thereby con-
tracted rheumatism in the most severe form. It
seemed to settle all over him ; he was brought
home, put to bed, and for more than eight weeks
could not move hand or foot. His agony was
such that he would scream if anyone came into
the room for fear they would touch him. His
knees were swollen to three times their natural
size. He was as near crazy as man could be, but
at the same time retained his faculties. Every-
thing was tried without benefit. In despair he
was taken to the Wolverhampton Hospital, where
he remained four weeks, at the end of which time
he was even worse than when admitted, and the
medical gentlemen who attended him at the
hospital told him that they could do nothing more
for him, that his case was hopeless, and he was
discharged as incurable. He was taken home,
where his wife persuaded him, as a last resort, to
try St. Jacob’s oil, which was procured from
Weaver’, chemist, 42 Dudley-road, Green remark-
ing at the same time, in a joking way, to his wife j
that if St. Jacobs oil cured him he would buy
her a new frock,” little expecting, however, that
he would have that pleasure. His wife then had
a double interest in curing her husband. She
applied half the contents of one bottle to his
knees, rubbing the parts vigorously for half an
hour when she left the room for a few minutes*
On returning she was surprised to hear Green say*
‘I shall have to buy a new frock, for I can turn
nyself and move my legs.” Continuing to use
this famous oil, Green commenced to improve,
and after using the contents of four bottles he was
out and on his cab in all weathers, and as well and
hearty as ever he was in his life. He was laid up
four months altogether, perfectly helpless, could
not move hand or foot. This was such an extra-
ordinary and almost unheard of cure, we deter-
mined to ascertain, if possible, what sort of thing
this St. Jacobs oil really was. We, therefore,
called upon Mr. Weaver, the chemist above
referred to, who stated at once what he knew of
Green’s case, and personally vouched for the above
statement, and stated further that, while Green’s
cure Avas perhaps the most remarkable, he knew
of numerous other extraordinary cases which had
come under his immediate attention Avhere St.
Jacobs oil had cured people of rheumatism and
neuralgia after every other means had been re-
sorted to Avithout avail. Mr. Weaver said the
sales of St. Jacobs oil Avere enormous ; people
invariably speak in the highest terms of its power
to conquer pain.
AN EXPERT’S OPINION.
gentleman writes to the Editor of the Water-
oury as follows ; “ I called on Mr. .T. E. Tucker,
jun., an expert watchmaker and engraver, who is
now with R. C. Richmond, of Glendive, Montana,
whom I had met before in St. Louis. Knowing
his hatred for the Waterbury watch, I was sur-
prised to see one of your signs, ‘ Waterbury
VYatches ’ hung in the window. Inquiry as to
this brought this in response : ‘ Yes, I have been
clown on the Waterbury watches all my life, and
I have never sold one half-dozen of them until
during the last month. My reasons for this
change are that I consider both the series ‘ J ’ and
‘Ladies’ Waterbury’ to be reliable watches. 1
have tested them carefully, honestly, and with all
the exactness that I, as a practical watch-maker
could, and I believe I do my customers justice by
selling them the Waterbury watches. I believe
the company to-day to be trustworthy friends of
the legitimate watchmaker; and I feel it to be
doing them a right, my customers a just act, and
my country a Messing, by discountenancing Swiss
watches, and selling instead the Waterburys.”
“ I have in constant use on the railroad here
nine Series ‘ J besides thirty two Rockford, Elgin,
and Waltham watches. Last month the nine
Waterburys made an average of 13.5 seconds, not
one being over 34 seconds off. The other watches
averaged 43 to 12 seconds. The Waterburys were
at a cost of odol each, the other watches from
25d0l to 125d01. We carry all kinds of watches,
but ‘ honesty is the best policy ’ always. I like
the Waterburys; they have been weighed and not
found wanting. I have decided to recommend
them, and to sell all of them I can.”

Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism.—Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne'have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,’
the Capital of West Australia.

Another tale of woman’s treachery and
guile. At the Carlton (Yic.) police-court an in-
dignant looking female swarmed into the witness-
box and swore that the dejected male trash in the
dock had gone off with an overcoat and a dog, and
had thereby left her hearth desolate ; and she
demanded gore in consequence and demanded it
in large capitals. Then the cast-off remnant
asked to be allowed to explain. He said he
wasn’t married to the bounding vision of loveliness
whose voice had just filled the court; but he sort
of cruised round the premises. She belonged to
another man, but the other man was away, and
he hung : about off and on in a friendly manner ;
dur ng his absence. She regularly lent him the
overcoat,, and he wore it till he thought it was his
own; and he was like a father to the dog. He
also humped coal for the female who had so un-
justifiably gone back on him, and now her treachery
had blighted him and he had shrivelled like - a
spider that had sat down on a red-hot shovel. He
considered that woman was a failure and that the
flying spectacle of feminine joyousness was mostly
stuffed with wool, or fluff of some kind, and that
when it wasn’t stuffed it was flat-chested and un-
interesting. He had doubts about the nair
of the whole female community, and after
the way his simple, childlike faith had been
trodden -on he wouldn’t be surprised if the
lady whom he had trusted with his fresh
young affections had a glass eye. All the
pleasant allusions of life had fallen with a dull
thud, and he could no more pick them up again
than he could balance a cataract on his ear. He
had kept the plaintiff in victuals for several
months, and had been a brother to her, also a hus-
band and a father, and an uncle from the country,
and at the very first disagreement between them
she had proceeded to sit on him in a heartless
manner. Such conduct was calculated to make
1 his hair—but just at this point the Bench dis-
charged him and cast him out into the street
because it couldn t stand his eloquence any longer,
and the plaintiff followed him out, and they
became reconciled and embraced each other
tenderly on the pavement.
