Land of the Crocodile.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When the late Henry Jordan was Minister
fur Lands in Queensland, he introduced a scheme
of “village settlement,” whereby settlers could
live together in a central township and fulfil con-
ditions at the same time on neighbouring selections.
Jordan wrote to official and unofficial people in
various parts of the colony to ask their opinions
on the prospects of success. Among the official
correspondents was J. Starcke, Inspector of
Northern .Surveys, a man possessed of- a complete
set of opinions entirely his own, and addicted to
expressing the same with unaffected candour, re-
gardless of consequences. Starcke was asked to
say if he thought the Russell River a suitable site
for a Village Settlement, and his report is said to
have embodied the following passage ; “ I hud
that in places like the Russsll River, one-
third of the settlers die from fever, one-
third are drowned by floods, and the other
third are eaten by the blacks. Under these
circumstances I do not think Village Settlement
is likely to be a great success !” And it gradually
dawned upon Jordan that “ under those circum-
stances” it might certainly be just as well to let
the Russell River Village Settlement stand over
in the meantime for further consideration.
# # #
And this reminds me that a surveyor once
wrote from the Johnstone River to say that “ the
blacks have stolen all my provisions and also
sampled two of my Chinamen !"
• # *
You have heard of the good-natured Rev.
William Ridley (once editor of Sydney E. News),
who published a book on “Kamilaroi” and other
dialects about 15 years ago ? Ridley, in the
delightfully unreasoning simplicity of pious en
thusiasm, used to walk in among a tribe of myalls
whose religious instruction had been previously
restricted to timber-getters’ oaths, and without
any formal notice, open out on the astonished
sons of the forest with a fragmentary and dis-
jointed lecture on the Atonement and the Fall of
Adam. Then the missionary rode away again into
dim space, seated on the ridge-pole of an eccentric
steed, that looked as if its chief diet consisted of
sida retusa and worn-out tracts, leaving the simple
myalls to dispose of the theological problem by
the intelligent remark —“My word, thafella
mitchanarree cabon —cranky
# #
Mr. Ridley once called at old Major
North’s station on the Upper Brisbane River
during sheep-washing, and preached as usual to
the station blacks. Next day he was standing on
the edge of the wash-pool observing the proceed-
ings. A tall myall was advancing trom the rear
escorting a powerful and bellicose ram. No sooner
did this intelligent animal observe the simple-
minded parson, looming large and dark on the
horizon, than all the worst traits of his nature
were stirred up from the remotest depths. Then
the “Fixed Ideas” united to make all the material
forafirst-c’asscatastrophe: an unsuspecting servant
of the Lord, a blood-thirsty ram, and an Australian
savage with the keen sense of humour peculiar to
his race. The savage released the ram, the ram
made one wild bound forward and struck the mis-
sionary in the rear with an impelling force
estimated at lialf-a-ton ; the missionary rose in a
parabolic curve and descended head-first among
the performers in the washpool, followed by an
astonished ram turning a somersault under the
impetus of his charge. And the smile which rose
to Heaven from the assembled blacks has seldom
been rivalled and probably never excelled.
# # *
Recently L met an old colonist who said
- he saw Leichhardt at Ipswich in 1843 “ a-thin,
spare man with along, serious face.” lie was
also present when Hargreaves, the gold-discoverer,
passed through Ipswich in 1852, and sold his outfit
at the Queen's Arms Hotel.
» • *
Many of your readers nave heard of
"Jimmy Morrill,” who was 17 years among the
Burdekin blacks. His real name was James
Murrells. He was a native of Malden, in Essex,
and the only final survivor of the barque Peruvian,
wrecked on the n rth coast in March, 184(i. The
survivors landed at Cape Cleveland, near Towns-
ville, and were kindly treated by the blacks, but
all died with n a couple of years except J. Murrells,
who was recovered from the, blacks in 1863. When
the first Townsville, land was sold at Bowen,
Murrells was present at the sale. Some one sug-
gested to the auctioneer to knock down a certain
allotment to him at upset price, and Murrells be-
cime the possessor for £8 10s. This allotment was
sold by “Johnny Parkes,” of Townsville, acting for
Murrells’ son,' for £lO,OOO, three years ago.
Murrells himself died on Oet. 30, 1865.
# * *
When “Jimmy Morrill” first came in to a
newly-formed station on the Burdekin River
(native name, “Mailmall”) he sang out to a
threatening hutkeeper, “Don’t shoot! I’m a
British object! ” He had nearly forgotten his
own language. There were two stockmen who
had been out on the run and shot several blacks.
These stockmen heard some, days after that
Murrells would recognise them and that they
would be arrested and charged with the murders.
Their hearts fell into their boots ! One went to
the other, and said, “ Tom ! my best brown horse
intends to be a hundred miles from here to-
morrow night! I don’t like to leave this place,
but I won t let the horse go away by himself.”
And Tom said, “All right, Charley, we were
old friends, and are not going to separate now.
I’ll go with yon !”
Under the rirrumstanns, this Pylades and
Orestes friendship was touching to behold 1
# # &
One meets people with strange experi-
ences. Mr. K. F. Burchett, council clerk at
Southport, the Brisbane watering-place*, is a
“Chevalier of Pius the Ninth,” and fought as a
lieutenant in the Pontifical Zouaves at Mentana,
Monte Libretto, Earncse and Nerola. He was
among the “Volunteers of the West” in the
Franco-Prussian War, and one of the surviving
60 from the 300 who went into that deadly
charge at Patte, near Orleans. He mounted
guard at the Pincio, in Rome, with Major Lewis^
the present governor of the Brisbane jail; and
Don Alfonso De Bourbon, brother of Don Carlos,
slept in the next bed as a private soldier, Burchett
doubtless has a great difficulty in restraining his
old-time thirst for blood, when listening' now to
the aldermanic debates, and expostulating with
clam-headed ratepayers.
# * *
A brief romance from real life. We first
behold the hero as a lawyer’s clerk, in Melbourne,
under the name of Jack H 1. Feeling a desire
for excitement and change of air he appropriated
his employer’s funds. That too amiable employer
gave him 24 hours to remove outside the city
boundary, and as far beyond as possible in the
shortest recorded time. We see him next cheeking
a consignment of Honolulu oranges on a wharf iu
San Francisco. He then looms up on the sky-line
in a solicitor’s office in London, where lie again
hankers after his employer’s cash and suddenly
vanishes once more trackless into blue immensity.
Years pass away, iu the usual manner of those
measured periods of time, and a gentleman, ac-
companied by a lady, is seen driving in a hand
some pair-horse carriage on a Queensland race-
course. One who knew him iu his early days ap-
proached and addressed him by the old name. Both
name and acquaintanceship were promptly denied.
He bore another name, was a prominent citizen, and
occupied an important public po-ition. He met
the old acquaintance next day, admitted being
identical with J.H., the ex-lawyer’s- clerk, and
implored silence on his antecedents. The silence
was granted, but his old propensity clung-to him
still, and ultimately triumphed. His final per
formance was a painful shock to the community
in which he had been trusted and honoured. He
is now serving a sentence of seven years for
forgery !

A bullock-team was on the way to Gayn
dab, and on the top a coop containing a prize
turkey, consigned to a Gayndah bird-fancier. The
bullock-driver was out turkey-shooting with two
surveyors, Oharleton and another. Charleton
THE NEW “SYDNEY" THEATRE, CASTLE REACH I STREET, SYDNEY.
fired at a plain turkey, which flew away towards
the camp. Chai leton imagined that he saw the
wounded bird perch on the dray, and stalked up
in the dusk of the evening and shot him dead.
The bullock-driver walked up and picked up the
dead game. “ Oh, my poor turkey ! was all
the gentle oxen-persuader found strength to utter.
The prize turkey had escaped from the coop and
was standing on top of the waggon. Hence Ids
untimely death !'
MAROOGALINE.
The Melbourne Tkleoravh curses with ,a
great, prolific curse at the proposal to open public
libraries on Sunday, but it is careful to state that
it is no sin for the parson's cook to labour on tin*
day of rest in order to prepare a hot dinner for t he
apostle. If it had said anything el-e there would
have been a very hungry and indignant apostle
round at the office to stop his paper first thing
next morning. The preacher is generally well up
in all matters relating to nourishment, and he will
go to the front of the battle and stay there if any
one tries to lay a sacrilegious hand on his half-ton
of victuals. The person who goes forth to rend
his'hot. Sunday dinner from the bearer of good
tidings had better go cased in mail and ready to
die in mutilated fragments in the last ditch ; and
if there is any more cheering and energetic spec-
tacle on earth than one small teacher throwing six
armed men down the front steps in defence of his
stuffed Sabbath turkey we can only say that that
other spectacle has never yet come under our
notice.

“ On bat le rappel L-hautl”—Alfred Murof.k.
Since men are not fashioned like cattle,
They struggle and suffer and sin;
They push to the front of the battle.
Determined to conquer or win.
Do I'seek to diminish that ardour?
1 answer you, mournfully, No :
But the battle gets harder and harder—
Listen! On bat,
On bat le rappel Ui-haut!
Our little Bohemian legion
Expected no conqueror’s arch,
And we trudged through a desolate region,
And often were faint on the march.
Bound the bivouac fire we assembled,
That fire was uncertain and low';
As our eyelids w ere closing, we trembled —
Listen ! On bat,
On bat le rappel la-huut!
There was one of our band whom we cherished
The youngest, the purest, the best
In the frost of the night-time he perished,
Going quietly home to his rest;
And we thought, as we buried our dear one,
And mournfully turned us to go,
That the summons was still sounding near one
Listen ! On bat,
On bat le rappel la-ha at!
() the joys of the past! The caresses,
The kisses from lips that are cold,
The eyes that were blue, and the tresses
That waved with a ripple of gold-!
We have lived, we have.loved,, we have spoken
Hot words that set hearts in a glow ;
And now we are weary and broken —
Listen ! On hat,
On bat le rappel Id-kaut!
W. JEFFERY BROWSE.

CADBURY'S COCOA is closely allied to
milk in the large proportion of flesh-forming and
strength-sustaining elements that it contains. It is pre-
pared on the principle of excluding the superabundance
of fatty indigestible matter with which Cocoa abounds
supplying a refined thin infusion of absolutely pure
Cocoa, exhilarating and refreshing, for Breakfast, Lun-
cheon, Tea or Supper, giving staying power and im-
parting new life and vigour to growing children, and
those of delicate constitutions.
The King that is to Come.
(REVISED FOR THE BULLETIN BY THE AUTHOR,
AND INSCRIBED TO THE AUSTRALIAN LABOUR
FEDERATION.)
•■He shall give justice to the poor of the people-he
i...M save the children of the needy and shall break in
pieces the oppressor.”—72nd Psalm.
O the Leader of the Future, will he come to us as
Thecldefof conquering cohorts ’mid the roll of
battle-drums? . , T .
Will he come as came the victor m the Roman
streets of old? .
Will he come in regal raiment with a coronet of
A crown of gleaming gold,
A crown of gleaming gold,
Will he come in regal raiment with a crown of
gleaming gold ?
No! his hand will bear no sceptre and his brow
will wear no crown, , , .
And no robe of royal purple shall be worn his
loins around; , , . .
But his throne shall be the hearts of men, his
voice will utter words
To thrill the listening millions like reverberating
chords—
Like reverberating chords,
Reverberating chords,
To thrill the mighty millions like reverberating
chords.
A Hero Vindicator, lie will come at first as comes
The music of a clarion-call above the throb of
drums, .
Then let us open wide our ears to hear the Miriam
That’ll^tell desponding millions—“ Lo ! thy
leader’s marching on,
Thy leader’s marching on,
Thy leader’s marching on,
Ho! gather mighty millions, Lo ! thy leader’s
marching on.”
No"nimbus will illume his brow—no jewelled
diadem, , ,
Yet his fame will shine resplendent as the lustre
of a gem, .
And the Emperor of the Universe upon this man
will smile; .
Who’ll beard the Mighty and the Proud like Moses
by the Nile —
Like Moses by the Nile,
Like Moses by the Nile,
He’ll beard the Mighty and the Proud, like Moses
by the Nile.
He’ll view the hosts of tyrants with their murder-
flags unfurled,
And his heart will bleed in anguish at a subjugated
world, .
Then his tongue will sound a bugle-blast—his
hand will grasp the pen,
Each word will be an army-corps—each line a
million men—
Each line a million men,
Each line a million men,
Each word will be an army-corps, each line a
million men.
Aye ! he’ll raise his hand to Heaven, and he’ll
speak the word of might.
That will scatter butcher-armies as the morning
scatters night, _
And the chiefs of iron legions and the robbers of
the poor,
Shall furl their blood-soaked banners to rule and
rob no more,
To rule and rob no more,
Rule and rob no more,
Shall furl their blood-soaked banners to rule and

His voice will sway the nations, and Truth his

To shear the mail of mighty ones, to set the cap-
tive free,
Yet he may come in lowly guise, with neither
trump nor drum;
Then let us work and watch for him—the king
tliat is to come!
The king tliat is to come,
The king that is to come,
Then let us work and hope for him, the king that
is to come ! ■
ARTHUR DESMOND.
New Zealand.
An Enthusiastic Blue Ribboner.
It happened, some considerable time since, that a
wild, dare-devil spirit tempted us to a ten minutes’
trip by rail on a suburban line. We were re-
clining gracefully in a corner of a first-class com-
partment, closely wrapped in our meditations (for
the air was chilly), when a long, thin, elderly
gentleman climbed—with apparent difficulty—
into the carriage, and sank into the seat imme-
diately opposite to us. A casual glance disclosed
that the stronger was rubicund and cheerful of
countenance ; and was, moreover, gazing upon us
with an earnest intentness which seemed to
denote that he was favourably impressed with our
appearance, and would like to become friendly.
But to people possessing an agreeable personnel,
such displays of affectionate interest are by no
means rare (incidentally we may remark that we
have favourably impressed scores of strangers—
sometimes to their sorrow), and we had relapsed
into our train of thought and almost forgotten the
existence of our rubicund companion, when a
cheery voice broke the silence with— “ Well, have
you been to any of these gatherings of Booth and
Glover’s ?” Mechanically we replied that our
time had been too much taken up with “ Harmy ’•
work for us to enjoy that pleasure. “Splendid
fellows,” continued the stranger, “you ought to
join us ; Glover’s a particular friend of mine, too.
I’d like to introduce you. Interested, in spite of
ourselves, by the heartiness of our elderly adviser,
we showed our teeth in token of amity, and
observing, for the first time, a bit of blue
ribbon pinned conspicuously on his coat, hazarded
the conjecture that he had “signed.” “Oh!
yes, yes ; it’s never too late to mend, you
know. Splen-did fellows! splen-did fellows !”
This principle, we observed, had been rigidly
carried out with: the garments of the old gentle-
man ; still we liked him none the worse for his
thrift. There was, too, a something genial about
his trick of dwelling on his words which encou.
raged us to inquire “ if he drank as much now as
in the old days before he was converted ?” “Oh !
the usual, the u-usual, you know,” he said, as he
offered us a pinch of snuff—“a few glasses of ale
I’m always very moderate.” Pleased and touched
by the ingenuousness of his reply, we next ven.
tured a query as to whether he had taken the
pledge immediately on his entry into the fold. A
moment afterwards we regretted our curiosity, for
this last question so evidently awakened painful
recollections in the mind of our honest old friend.
His face grew unwontedly grave, and his voice
dropped to a sad, confidential whisper. “It was
very unfortunate,” he murmured; “but directly
after signing, I was seized with a nasty, sinking
sensation here (placing a trembling hand upon his
waistcoat) “ and I had to go out at once and get a
little drop of brandy.” “Much after that?” we
qwired sympathetically. ‘ 1 Ao-o, only a glass or two
of ale —my usual, my w-usual, you know.” An
unmistakeable hiccup after the last word warned
us that the jolting of the train had acted unfavour-
ably upon the adherent of temperance principles,
and having now reached our destination (for there
had been no accident that evening) we bade him
an affectionate adieu. “Sleep over it,” said the
affectionate one, as he held our hand in his
beery grasp, “ and make up your mind to join us.
They are both splenaWd fellows.” As we walked
home, ruminating on the stranger’s words, we felt
that many of the most ardent “ tee-totallers ’ are
not so narrow-minded, after all.
The Genesis of Marmalade.
Nellie Stewart's Bouquet.
The following straightforward-looking let-
ter is clipped from the columns of the Central
Australian and Bourke Telegraph. If the
facts of the case are as stated, the letter sheds a
radiant effulgence upon the manner in which
presentations to public personages are got up, it
explains fully the unexpected benevolence that
sometimes animates professional personages, and
further elucidates the manner in which the slush-
and-slobber-vendor of the “ jaynial ” order, who is
afflicted with a mania for being always before the
public, manages to attain and hold that promin-
ence :
To the Editor of the Central Australian.
Sir,—As my recent presentation at Sydney Theatre
Royal seems to have drawn forth a good deal of unfair
criticism, I think it due to my constituents and myself
that I should explain how I was drawn into this little
episode. The following are the facts:—Last Saturday
week the Honourable Daniel O’Connor, who is a friend of
Miss Nellie Stewart and Mr. Musgrove, the manager, sent
for Mr. Davis and me that he wished to see us (sic). I
was not in town, so Mr. Davis went alone. Mr. O'Connor
informed him that .Miss Nellie Stewart’s company
was to perform at the Theatre Royal next Satur-
day night, and that it would be a most grace-
ful act to present Miss Stewart with a floral
gift on behalf of the residents of Bourke as a mark of their
appreciation for her noble generosity in first giving £SO
and afterwards the proceeds of a benefit amounting to
£250 to the Bourke Flood Relief Fund. Mr. Davis in-
formed Mr. O’Connor that he would speak to his colleague,
Mr. Waddell, on Monday with reference to the matter and
see what he thought of it. On Monday I met Mr. Davis,
and in the course of conversation he mentioned what had
occurred and asked me what I thought of : the matter. I
at once said that I would not undertake the duty, and
Mr. Davis agreed with me that while It would be a graceful
act, still it was unnecessary. I thought no more about it
till next day when I got a message from Mr. O’Connor
that he wanted to see me. I called on him a few hours
after and he introduced the matter as he had done With
Mr. Davis, and pressed me to undertake the duty, men-
tioning at the same time that two leading gentlemen
whom he named would give a guinea towards buying the
present. He also informed me that all arrangements were
made and that a box would be placed at the disposal
of myself and any friend I liked to bring, free of cost.
Still disliking to undertake so delicate a duty, 1 left him
without giving any promise that I would do it. The next
day I discussed the matter with my colleague Mr. Davis ;
I would have also seen Mr. Willis but was informed that
he was away somewhere in the country. On chatting the
matter over Mr. Davis mentioned that he could not re-
main in town till Saturday night. I at once said that that
would be a good excuse to put Mr. O’Connor off what he
seemed so intent on seeing done. So I sat down and
wrote Mr. O’Connor a letter saying that Mr, Davis had to
leave town before Saturday, and that it must therefore
be postponed, thinking I would thereby put Mr. O’Connor
off altogether. I had the letter immediately sent to him.
About an hour after he again sent for me and pressed me
strongly to make the presentation, at the same time again
saying that all arrangements were made and that I had
nothing to do but to attend the theatre on Saturday night
and make the presentation and he would be there, too. I
then consented and asked what would be my share of
the cost, which Mr. O’Connor informed me. On Friday
afternoon I called to give him my cheque, but finding
he was out I wrote a few lines enclosing the cheque, &o.
On Saturday night, when I arrived at the theatre in com-
pany with my wife and some friends, one of those con-
nected with the theatre met us at the door and, on
learning my name, at once conducted me round to a box
which he said had been reserved for us, and this gentle-
man informed me that it was a floral crown which I had
to present, and that Mr. Musgrove, the manager, had all
arrangements made and would inform me when the proper
time came to make the presentation. Up to this time 1
thought that I would be asked to make the presentation
at the intermission between the acts when the light was
turned on the audience and they would not expect the
opera to be going on, and would consequently not feel
impatient at the time occupied—and this was where tne
management made a mistake in not having it so arranged.
However, a few minutes before the end of the second
act Mr. Musgrove came to me and informed me
that he had arranged that at the first fall of
the curtain I should take my place on the stage
and make the presentation when the curtain rose.
I at once realised (no notice of the presentation
being given in the programme, and my appearance on the
gav and festive scene before the act had closed) that the
audience under such circumstances would regard the
presence of a stranger with astonishment, and I accord-
ingly commenced by saying that “As the senior member
for Bourke I had much pleasure in making the presenta-
tion,” &c., &c. This was the “political allusion ” which
has drawn forth such strong condemnation from one or
two of the writers on the press who were no doubt labour-
ing under the delusion that I had undertaken the presen-
tation for my own gratification and pleasure. These are
the simple facts of my escapade. It was the first, and I can
safely say the last, time that I allowed myself to be drawn
into anything against my own inclination and judgment.
However, in justice to Mr. O’Connor, who performed a
similar act on behalf of the Bourke residents when in
Melbourne at the time of the flood, I am sure he had no
object in view but to see one of those graceful acts per-
formed for which he is so noted. In conclusion I may say
that my first appearance on the stage not having met
with the success I could have wished, I shall retire from
the profession, and a word of warning 1 may add, that
should anyone be so daring as to ever ask me to make a
similar presentation, be he the most distinguished gentle-
man in the land or not, there will be (as Artemus Ward
says) “ a burial in his family” shortly after.—Yours, fee.,
T. Waddell.— Sydney, 22nd July, 1890.

Seldom has Melbourne society been so
stirred to its centre as by the death of the bright
and attractive only daughter of General Tulloch,
almost snatched by the King of Terrors away
from a ball. The General is as popular as a soldier
can be in an Australian community, and seems to
have Wolseley ideas of making the force truly
national.
Of the late Matt Morgan, artist, Alfred
Trumble thus writes in the N. Y. Epoch :
To the once famous Tomahawk, of London, his wjt and
art provided the keen blade, while Arthur a’Beckett,
as editor, gave it its handle. The Tomahawk described
itself as “A Saturday Journal of Satire.” It astonished
London for theflrst time at the beginning of May, 1857.
It consisted of .eight pages of text, a cartoon and a cover,
and cost twopence. In its sixteenth issue the artist began
to use his now familiar signature. Matt Morgan. The
Tomahawk cut deep from its very beginning. In No.
■8 began the terrific attacks on the Prince of Wales, with a
double-page cartoon, showing the prince as Hamlet, on
the battlements, breaking away from the restraint of his
friends to follow the phantom of George the Fourth, with
the words, “I’ll follow thee 1” As the prince was just
then modelling his life after that of the debauchee
son of George the Third, this bold burlesque to’d
immensely. But the sensation was as nothing beside that
aroused by No. 14, where a double-page cartoon, called “A
Brown Study,” printed in black on a brown tint, displayed
the Queen’s valet, John Brown, leaning against the back
of the empty throne, with a clay-pipe in his right hand,
his left, at his hip, reaching stealthily out for the crown,
which stood in a case on a stand behind hun, and the
British lion, aroused by the threat of theft, growling as he
climbed the steps of the dais leading to the seat of power.
This fierce and savage hack at a scandal of the day, which
did not spare the Queen herself, but which her loyal sub-
jects scarcely dare discuss under their breaths, made the
whole nation shudder and buy the paper. One result of
this cartoon was that the Tomahawk got a dose of official
attention that dulled its edge to the sacred privilege of
the throne for many weeks to come.
A fascinating piece of verse appears in a
recent number of that cultured and graceful pub-
lication, the Sydney Evening News. It is en-
titled “The Rivulet,” and is signed “A Milo
Bennett ’’—-and here is a hunk of it: —
Sometimes dancing on right merrily,.
Never spreading pain or woe ;
And then again flowing surlily,
Needing sunshine, like the Po,
Men are like the little brooklet.
Onward rushing to their fate ;
Both need sunshine to endure it,
And that early not too late.
As we have never heard that Mr. Alfred Bennett,
the accomplished managing-proprietor of our
esteemed contemporary, had a Milo in his name,
we may be wrong in attributing to him the author-
ship of this charming lyric, in which case we are
open to make the frankest and most handsome of
apologies. However that may be, we are certain
Mr. Bennett is one of the very few men •in Aus-
tralia capable of penning so enthralling a set of
verses, and it is likely enough only Mr. B.’s
abnormal modesty which in this ease causes
him to suppress most of the Alfred and
to give to the Milo an unusual prominence. The
old original Mho, we happen to remember, was a
powerful man who carried a calf on the day of its
birth and thereafter carried it every day until it
became a full-grown bull, when he dashed it to
the ground and killed it with his fist; and though
Mr. Alfred Bennett may never have been seen
carrying a bull, we have known his paper to carry
from time to time a large assortment of prize
animals of that description. Again, the talented
author may, after all, turn out to be one
of the junior members of the Bennett family,
in which case we cordially felicitate Mr. Alfred
Bennett on being more or less directly con-
nected with an entity who would appear to have
in his golden youth a first mortgage at sixty per
cent., so to speak, on the proudest laurels of
poetic genius.
A Melbourne lady complains rather vio-
lently of the conduct of another lady who took
advantage of her temporary absence to call in and
die at her house. She didn’t know the other lady
at all, and she considered her conduct as dis-
courteous. No doubt this feeling is natural
enough, but still if a lady is out walking and feels
a sudden desire to die right off it is evident she
must go somewhere to do it. In the case of a
gentleman it might be reasonable enough to expect
him to go down a quiet street and die, or to pass
away on the grass in a park; but when a lady
knocks at the door and asks to be allowed to come
in and perish for a few minutes, no right-minded
person could well refuse the request. Of course, if
the visitor intends to finish off with some violent
complaint which will lead her to jump all over the
place in her last moments, and break pretty nearly
every perishable article in the house, and heave
her last sigh on top of a heap of slaughtered
furniture that cost £7O when it was new, there
might be some ground for objection; but
such cases are rare and they can be easily
diagnosfd in time, for a violent patient of this
order generally kicks the door in instead of ringing
the bell. In the majority of instances, however,
the -sufferer merely desires to sit down and breathe
the word “mother” in a minor key, and then
float away, and under such circumstances the
householder who would tell her to go and die in a
threepenny omnibus or a pastrycook’s shop, and
then slams the door, is little better than a brute.
Even if the invalid hollers a good deal in her last
fleeting respirations that is no Reason why any
Christian person should refuse to accommodate
her for a few minutes, so long as she is content to
decease in a chair and doesn’t want to overturn
the vvashstand and then wind up by calling for
brandy with her head inserted in 'the ashes under
the grate; and any quietly-disposed person, who
wishes to die either in this office or at the sub-
editor’s private residence, will always find a hard
seat, a prayer-book, and a glass of pump-water at
her disposal.
They are a slow, tranquil, drowsy race who
inhabit Christchurch (N.Z.), the City of the Plains.
When a man calls on his best girl there, he gene-
rally sits and gazes at the album for a month or
six weeks while he is making up his mind what to
say next, and when he leaves he seldom looks in
again for three years. It is quite a common thing
for a person to be grown up in Christchurch before
his friends are aware that he is born, and, as a
rule, he is dead ten years before his nearest relative
hears of it. A Christchurch citizen never loses a
train in the ordinary acceptation of the term, but
sometimes he has to hurry a good deal to catch
the train that starts a fortnight after the one he
was aiming at. It is very rarely that anything
happens over there, for any kind of event that
won’t keep is sure to go bad befcr ' it can take
place, and consequently an incident which occurred
suddenly a few weeks ago created a slight stir.
It was evening, and two fond hearts were
gathered together on a sofa. They had arranged
to be married in thirteen years or so, and
were full of blissful contentment. The pet
monkey belonging to the man next door was
sitting on the roof and combing his ear with
his hind leg, but they were not conscious of his
existence. But after a while the cat from the end
house came roaming over the tiles, and an un-
pleasantness arose between the two animals. It
ended badly, and in a few seconds the monkey
started out for the chimney and for home, but in
his excitement he escaped down the wrong flue.
A few seconds later an awful thing, all shriek and
hair and curly tail, came scrambling and sneezing
into a grate where be wasn’t expected, with a cloud
of soot following after him, and fell into the fire.
He can.e out of it again quite suddenly, though,
and did an affrighted spin round the room, clutch-
ing at the girl’s back-hair as he took a flying jump
off the small of her back and made for the side-
board. He took nearly a pound of the hair with
him, and its late owner took refuge under the
sofa. Then the young man rose solemnly and
prepared to do something in about ten days
or a fortnight in defence of his future
bride. The scared ape skimmed along the
mantelpiece, and raked down £5-worth of
ornaments, and his enemy started after him
with the velocity of an iron safe. By the time he
arrived at the mantelpiece the animal was sitting
on top of a picture at the other end of the room,
and when the picture fell it made more noise than
a panorama. Naturally enough, this put new
rapidity into the singed quadruped, and it escaped
into the interior of the sideboard, but when its
pursuer came up and turned the sideboard face
downwards on the floor to make it a prisoner, he
only reduced the contents to unspeakable chaos,
for the invader was already flying down the stair.
Nevertheless, the youth sat holding the capsized
furniture down with a firm hand for three-quarters
of an hour, till his girl took courage to come out
from under the sofa and rush gradually into her
own room to put on some more hair. Then
an affrighted wail announced that the monkey was
there also, and while the hero still held down the
overturned sideboard the animal made its final
retreat through a window carrying her best bonnet
impaled on its tail. It is characteristic of the
slowness of the slowest city on earth that oven then
the infatuated youth continued to hold on to the
ruined furniture for 48 hours longer under a firm
conviction that he had an animal of some kind
imprisoned inside, and when he found his mistake
he was so mortified that he went away and is not
expected to call again for two years. In conse-
quence of the alarm created by this incident it has
been considered advisable to postpone the wed-
ding to some date a little further on in the next
century.
We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which shoo'd boa guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with.

Tales of “The System."
[No. IV.; Absalom Day’s Promotion.]
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The sound of a bell came floating on the
pellucid atmosphere to the ears of the dense, wait-
ing crowd.
“ That ain’t the Tench clapper yet, surely ?” ex-
claimed a bleary-eyed old ticket-of-leave man
who stood in the front-rank, so close to the files of
the military guard that his noisome breath dulled
the pipe-clayed polish on the nearest private’s
shoulder-belt.
“Yer ought’r know, oP chap,” sneered an
“F.S.” (free-by-servitude) man. Yer slep’ of’n
’nuff in the ol’ lumber-yard dorm’tories.”
“Wot stoopids ye must be as not to know as
that’s the bell of the Beagle as is striking eight
bells !” broke in a third person whose assumption
of superiority, evidenced in many airs of dress
and manner, was based on the circumstance that
he was still “ free ” —a state or condition which, if
his physiognomy possessed any value as an index,
rather reflected on the vigilance of the authorities.
And then he proceeded to inform his precious
companions that the Beagle was a King’s ship, “as
was a-goin’ round the world a-takin’ soundin’s an’
a-collectin’ of shells.”
“Well,” said the T.-L. man, “th’ curipwsest
thing as they’ll see aboard of her will be this ’ere
exeewshun. ”
Which remark, though somewhat ungrammati-
cal, was certainly not far from the truth.
“ Eight bells, was it ?” continued the pass-
holder i then th’ Tench will give tongue soon.”
His bristly lips had not ceased to vibrate before
the sound they were waiting for was borne on the
aromatic mountain breeze.
“One ”
The deep, solemn tone of Trinity Church-
bell—the St. Sepulchre’s of Hobart Town-
swayed the chattering, jostling, jesting, crowd
into silence and stillness for a moment
only. Before the second toll of the pass-
ing bell reached the throng the chattering became
excited and furiotis talk, the jests more biting and
brutal, the jostling an angry struggle for a fore-
most place. The soldiers guarding the vacant
space in front of the monstrous scaffold which ran
almost the full length of the gaol-facade, were
ordered to face about and press back the surging
mass with their extended firelocks. The tolling of
the bell had communicated a passion like the mad-
ness of fever to the veins of the people. The
madness of fever, say we ? Better, the delirium
of blood-thirst. The people of Hobart Town were
about to enjoy a rare treat, and their lips were
dry for it. “ Execution Mondays ” were common
enough in the Van Diemen’s Land calendar, and
it was an exceptional day of the series when only
one doomed wretch swung off the platform and
into the Mystery which men in their ignorance
label Eternity. But it was still more exceptional
to have a round dozen of hangings on one day, at
the one hour, and in the one place. In fact, there
was nothing but a vague tradition to encourage
the notion that the event which had occasioned
the assembling of the crowd was not absolutely
unprecedented in the Island history. The circum-
stance was, anyhow, quite exceptional in the ex-
perience of the existing authorities, and they had
been in some doubt as to whether they ought not
divide the 12 condemned left for execution by the
last Court of Oyer and Terminer and General
Gaol Delivery into two batches of six each. The
desire, however, to afford to the criminal element
of the population a peculiarly “ impressive warn-
ing ” prevailed, and, accordingly, the simul-
taneous execution of the 12 was decided
upon. Moreover, it was also resolved by the
Executive Council—present, the Governor, the
Chief Justice, the Attorney-General, the Colonial
Secretary, and the Archdeacon —that the event
should be surrounded with ceremonial details of
an unusual and xieculiarly impressive kind. As
the Clerk of the Council was entering upon the
minutes the programme for the day as approved
by the august body, he paused in his work to nod
acquiescence in the opinion expressed by the
sonorous accents of the Very Reverend the Arch-
deacon. “ The terrible exemplification,” said the
dignitary, “which Your Excellency, with the
advice of your Council, has prepared, of the
majesty of the Law and the heinousness of wrong-
doing, must beneficially impress the evil-disposed
among the people.” Vandemonia could not boast
of a Bishop in those days, or else this very proper
sentiment would have fallen from episcopal lips.
It was just the sort of sentiment that the superior
ecclesiastical functionary of the colony would be
impelled to utter by the obligations of his office as
representative of Christ. The Crown, ever mind-
ful of the moral and spiritual welfare of its sub-
jects, always desired the approval of the Church.
And, as the Crown paid the Church its stipend
and allowances, the approval was never withheld.
* # •
“ Ten — eleven .”
The noise caused by the crowd did not prevent
the little group of civil and military officials which
had gathered round the entrance-gate of the gaol
and Just underneath the extremity of the gallows
to which the ladder was attached, from counting
the pulsings of the minute-bell so far. A resonant
shout drowned the twelfth stroke,
“ They are coming,” cried the crowd. “ Look,
look !”
Into Murray-street from Campbell-street turned
a grim and gristy procession. Up the hill it came
slowly—so slowly. A regimental officer marched
at the head and gave the measured pace. He
recalled to himself as he walked that once before
he had done the same thing; but then it was for a
cortege of honour; now it was one of dishonour;
and his brow compressed and his lips set hard
as he cursed the fate which had made him, an
English gentleman, the marshal of a massacre,
the leader of a felon funeral. Behind him
his guard, four deep, with bayonets fixed and
ball-cartridge rammed well home. Behind the
soldiers one, two, three carts in succession, close
beside each walking, first a file of javelin-men, and
at a distance of “ intervals ” as many warders with
loaded muskets at the “ ready.” Behind the carts
again, more soldiers and more javelin-men, and,
last of all, the Sheriff, the Under-Sheriff, and the
Gaoler.
And the carts what of them ? Each was drawn
by a horse with rusty harness and dilapidated gear,
a wisp of yarn being substituted in more than one
place for a missing strap or part of a trace. Each
was driven by a grey-clad figure on whose clothing
stood the pronged symbol which proclaimed to all
men that the creature within it was a Thing—a
Chattel—a Number—anything, rather than a man.
Four other figures were also in the cart—no, let us
bo precise—we should say, five. And yet, per-
haps. wo would not bo wrong in saying that there
were four only- The fifth other man in each
vehicle was a functionary, and such importance as
he had was the reflection of the glory encom-
passing his four companions. The crowd which
lined the streets and which blackened the hill by
the Public Offices scarcely gave a thought to him.
It was upon the four upon whom it gazed—and
gloated. The four were principals in the ensuing
drama. The fifth man in each cart was simply an
accessory, indispensable, it is true —for he was
either the chief or an assistant-executioner —but
still an accessory.
* * *
The four in each cart were the cynosure of
every eye. They had the seats of distinction, for
each sat on his own coffin.
One —again to be precise—sat on the fragments
of his. Two prisoners in the House of Correction
workshops were constantly engaged in making
coffins, and, to save trouble and inconvenience in
emergencies, the shells were made to average sizes
and uniform patterns. Consequently it happened
very frequently that a condemned man found his
coffin a trifle too small for him. The law of ave-
rages was, however, respected, and the official
accounts balanced by some other wretch finding
his a trifle too large. On this exceptional occasion,
the particular shell assigned to John Bond, No.
440, per “ Asia” (1) was not only apparently too
small for him, but was not strong enough to grant
him the poor consolation of a comfortable seat to
the spot where he was to be hanged by the neck
till dead. As he cast his huge carcase upon the
lid on entering the cart, his weight had burst one
flimsy side from its fastenings, and, the lid slip-
ping, John Bond was deposited prematurely in
his shell, or, rather, on its planks. “ Why, Jack,
you are in a hurry to get in!” laughed James
Travis, No. 2320 per “Norfolk.” Travis was
always ready-witted. He had begged for the
first pick of the coffins that morning. “ He
wished to get one,” he said, “ which would just
suit him. He liked to be comfortable :” and he
had found an opportunity to jocosely lie full length
in his narrow bed, “so as to be sure he fitted it.”
Finding the measure satisfactory, he hilariously
flung his feet into the air. “ Taste and try before
you buy, ” said he. ‘ ‘ That’s been my rule of life,
and see how successful it has made me.”
Up the hill came the procession. Most of the
condemned laughed at and waved their hands to
the crowd. Only one of the twelve sat glum and
silent on his sombre perch. This was the youngest
of the lot —Absalom Day, who had taken advan-
tage of the comparative liberty he enjoyed as a
Sheriff’s javelin-man to break into a dwelling-
house, and, failing in his endeavour to throw the
blame on another convict, had been capitally sen-
tenced.
This was only Day’s third conviction, and, con-
sequently, he was not absolutely disgusted with
life. It Was a shame, he considered, to be “ top’d
off” so young; other men were allowed to live
when they had, perhaps, 30 or 40 convictions, in-
cluding two or three “ life’’-sentences recorded
against them.
Up the hill it came. The passing-bell tolled in
the ears of the Condemned. That was rather un-
pleasant, certainly. But, then, the bright morn-
ing sunshine glanced upon them genially, and the
breeze from Mount Wellington puffed piney per-
fume into their nostrils, and the crowd cheered
them, and the atmosphere of distinction was
about them. All these things were pleasant; and,
pleasantest of all, was not Freedom waiting for
them on top of the timber platform yonder ? Why
should they grieve ? Was not the balance in their
favour ? The door to which yon dangling ropes,
when looped, would prove the key-holes, could not
open upon a chamber of greater horror than that
from which they had emerged in the glorious sun-
light of their Execution Monday. Thus, thought
the Condemned : all, except Absalom Day.
* * *
Two members of the little group of
officials before-mentioned, mounted the steps of
the scaffold in order to gain a better view of the
procession’s progress.
“ Very impressive,” said Captain Grove, of His
Majesty’s th Fusiliers to Chaplain Ford.
“ Very impressive, indeed ! ”
“ Yes,” replied the Chaplain, “it was a happy
thought of the Council to order the men to spend
their last night at the Barracks instead of in the
gaol here. It enabled the Sheriff to organise a
very impressive—ah—ceremonial, indeed. Such a
spectacle must have the best possible effect upon
the convict class. ” He took from his fob a silver
snuff-box, and, having titillated his own organ,
tendered the box to the officer. “ Have a pinch,
Captain? I need a little stimulant on these
occasions. My duty is so trying ! ”
“Just so—thanks!” sympathetically answered
Captain Grose. “ It must be, I’m sure. I don’t
like hangings myself. I prefer a platoon at twenty
paces, you know.”
“ Ah, that would be too good for some of our
men,” smiled the parson. “ But I think it’s time
I put on my surplice.” He turned to descend
when a thought struck him. He looked up.
“ By the way, Captain, do you think that beam
is strong enough ? The weight will be rather much
to-day. I wouldn’t like any scandal; it would be
most unedifying.”
To assure themselves, the Chaplain and the Cap-
tain walked along the platform and inspected the
beam.
“ I think it will do,” said Chaplain Ford, taking
a second pinch. “ Have another ?”
“I think so. Ah —thanks!” The Captain
injected some more of the pungent stuff into his
nostrils. “Very good snuff, this of yours, sir.”
“ Yes, Tandy's best. I import it myself. And,
you know,” he whispered roguishly, “it doesn’t
pay duty !”
The Captain laughed, and they descended.
Chaplain Ford put on his surplice which his
prisoner clerk -a former mayor of an English city
—had brought from the vestry of the Tench
Chapel (“ Trinity ” was so called), and went to the
corner of the square, where the soldiers were
already forcing the people to clear a passage for
the entrance of the procession.
* # #
“ I am the resurrection and the life, saith
the Lord,” began Chaplain Ford, as he led the
way to the gallows. “He that believeth on me,
though he were dead, yet shall he live. ”
The rear-guard of the soldiers closed up round
the carts. One by one the Condemned got down.
Two by two they followed Parson Ford. They
left their coffins behind them; they would not
require them again for an hour or so. By their
side walked the javelin-men. Behind them, the
Sheriff and Under-Sheriff—and the executioner
and his assistants.
At the foot of the steps, Chaplain Ford moved
aside and mumbled some more portions of the
burial service. “Halt !” called the Sheriff. The
Condemned paused. The Sheriff beckoned to the
executioner and his assistants to pass on and
make-ready. The chief hangman went into the
gaol, came out a moment later with a bundle in
his hands, and ran lightly up the steps. He
passed along the platform, forming twelve little
heaps of calico and cordage as he traversed it. The
bundle was composed of the white caps and the
pinioning-eords. The Condemned looked on. and
smiled—all except Absalom Day. He shivered.
“ The Condemned will take their station as their
names are called out,” said the Sheriff.
“ Absalom Day," said Under-Sheriff Hopewell.
Day shuffled un the steps, trembling. Assist-
ant-Executioner Sharp assisted him to mount the
last, and guided him to the farther side of the
drop, where Chief Executioner Johnson received
him into his grasp.
The others mounted as their names were called
from the dread roll —some bravely, the others
with mere bravado ; all without assistance. They
were ranged in order of age—the youngest, Day,
on the extreme right of the platform as the crowd
faced it,
A word from Johnson to his subordinates, and
the task of pinioning was commenced. The ten-
sion of the crowd freed itself by a sigh ; the actual
drama had begun; the tedious prologue was
ended.
Johnson, being an expert, had taken Nos. 6
to 12 of the Condemned to pinion. Assistants
Sharp and Muggins, being only trainees, were
allotted but three each. They bungled their
proportion of the work as it was. Johnson
pinioned his six in .half the time taken by
each of the others for his three. The crowd did
not complain, however. Once the drama had
begun, the longer its performance the more pro-
longed their pleasures.
Johnson stopped in front of the Condemned to
“cap’’them. The assistants fell back to hand
the caps to him as he wanted them.
By a singular chance, the whole of the Con-
demned were Protestants. Hence, Chaplin Ford
alone was in requisition as the moment of doom
drew nigh. He was about to mount to say a few
final words —he had seen each man in his cell early
in the morning—when the Sheriff stopped him.
“Not yet, Mr. Ford, the death-warrants have
not been read. The Under-Sheriff will now please
read the warrants.”
Under-Sheriff Hopewell stepped up and Chap-
lain Ford stepped down petulantly. How could
he, the experienced prison Chaplain, and so fami-
liarised with the proper routine by frequent
attendance on similar occasions, have made such a
blunder ? Chaplain Ford was vexed with himself.
Under-Sheriff Hopewell opened the roll of parch-
ment sheets which he had carried under his arm.
He glibly recited the preambles as though he knew
them by heart—as was, indeed, probable. By the
time a man has repeated a form a couple of hun-
dred times he should know it by heart.
For each of the warrants the preamble was the
same. The “ orders, ” of course, varied in their
terms.
Beginning, on the left hand, with the oldest
criminal, the Under-Sheriff went down the line.
To John Bond, No. 440, per Asia (1), he read
the order of the Governor for his execution. His
crime was murder. And to John M‘Kenzie, No.
8764, Elphinstone, crime ... to Charles
Argyle, No. 687, Arab (2) . . . to Edward
M‘flavin, No. 12,351, Royal Sovereign, murder ;
to Robert Smith, free, attempt to murder; to
James Travis, No. 2320, Norfolk, rendering into
tallow a sheep suspected to be stolen (he had sold
the tallow for 7s. 6d., and the skin for Is.); to
Richard Bennett, No. 9244, Lord Lyndoch, rob-
bery under arms ; to Michael. Green, free, robbery
under arms ; to Phillip Grafton, No. 12,755, Pyra-
mtis, forgery and murder of arresting constable ;
to Joseph Madden, Tory, No. 14,009, murder; to
Peter Fyfe, No. 16,150, George 111., sheep-steal-
ing : and to Absalom Day, native, burglary with
violence. To all these he read the documents of
doom.
As the last clause —
These are therefore in His Majesty’s name strictly to
charge and command you the Sheriff of his Colony and
Dependency of Van Diemen’s Land that you see the said
sentence against the said Absalom Day duly put into
execution according to the tenor hereof. And for so doing
this shall he your sufficient warrant
was read, the crowd heaved another and deeper
sigh. The drama was approaching the climax.
Under-Sheriff Hopewell finished, Chaplain Ford
took up his duty.
“Now, raymen,”hesaid loudly, “rememberwhat
I told you this morning. God will forgive yon all
your sins if you really believe on Christ. Even
for such sinners as you, there is hope. Grafton,
yon are not listening, sir.” Over the crowd, soli-
tary in the blue vault, hung a fleck of snow-white
cloud. Grafton had been gazing on it. His fancy
had traced in the fleecy outlines the profile of his
dead mother’s face. He turned at the parson’s
words.
“My dear sir,” he replied—Grafton’s “Educa-
tion ” rank in the penal records was “ C ” {“ superior”)
—“ I have heard your remarks so often that I am
tired of them—and of you. But pardon me this
time —I will not offend again. He laughed, and
the others of the condemned joined in the chorus
of derision—all except Absalom Day.
“ Silence !” cried the Sheriff. The Chaplain
descended.
Once more the Sheriff spoke. “ Men,” he asked,
“have you anything to say? Now is your last
opportunity. You, Bond?”
A thrill penetrated the crowd as Bond flung
himself on his knees, and in so doing nearly pre-
cipitated himself from the gallows, as, his hands
and legs being pinioned, he had no power to
balance himself. Assistant Muggins pulled him
to his knees with a jerk.
Bond looked up to the smiling heavens, “I
wish to say this,” he shouted, “ that I thank God
my time has come at last. I killed Morrison be-
cause I wanted to die. ”
“ So do I thank you,” exclaimed the next man.
A dreadful “Hear, hear!” broke from the lips of
almost every man forming the doomed row.”
When it came to Travis’turn to speak, he said :
“I’m glad I’m going to be hanged, I am.
But I don’t thank God, for I don’t believe there
is any God. A God would have given me a
chance, and a chance I never had—no, never. Or
if He did give me one, the police and the beaks,
and the Com’troller took it from me. That’s all
I’ve got to say. Good-bye all.” He had spoken
with a clarion-like fullness of voice that reached
the outskirts of the now silent mass. As he
uttered his farewell he made a bow of mock-
dignity. From the centre of the throng came a
loud cheer, and a timid “ God have mercy on
you !”
The scandal of these proceedings determined
the Sheriff to prevent the remainder of the Con-
demned from speaking.
He motioned the executioner to begin “cap-
Eing.” In a second the pallid visage of Absalom
►ay was shrouded. The crowd, irritated at
losing “ a last dying speech ” from Grafton, yelled
in disappointment.
Johnson took the second cap from his assistant’s
hand, and was placing it on Fyfe's head when the
boy—he was only twenty—spoke to him.
“ It’s a long time, Dick, since we met, isn't it ?
A ;' so you’ve started in the wholesale butcherin’
line, have yer? Well, as you are an ol’ pal, I don’
min’ a-paternising yer !”
Johnson threw down the cap with an oath.
“ You’re right, Pete—it is wholesale butcherin’. I
don’ min’ one or two, but a dozen !” 1 ’m d d
if I hang so many—so there, Mr. Sheriff !”
And, defiantly, and never giving a thought to
the lashes which he must have known awaited
him, lie walked off the gallows.
Really, the drama was getting unexpectedly in
teresting-—to the crowd !
* * *
Not so to the Sheriff’. To say that ho was
rprised would be to use an absurdly-mild phrase.
He was stupefied by the executioner’s declaration.
The revolt of a hangman was absolutely unprece-
dented.
“ Do you mean it, Johnson?” ho gasped, at last.
Johnson did, and said so. “I’ll give you two
hundred ! ” said the Sheriff. The Sheriff didn’t
mean shillings,' or sovereigns. He meant lashes.
There was a minute’s conference between the
Sheriff and the other civil officials, and then the
former ordered the assistant-executioner to pro-
ceed with the awful business.
“Please, sir, I can’t,” said Muggins, “ I never
assisted before.”
“ Nor 1,” said Sharp.
The, Sheriff was dangerously near his wit’s end,
“111 ’sist, sir, if yes can get some im else to
knot th’ ropes, an’ yer’ll make my ticket a
pardon,” snokc a voice from the crowd. It was
the old ticket-of-leave man who proffered his
services.
The words suggested to the Sheriff that ho
should ask for volunteers from the throng. Ho
did so. None were forthcoming except the T.L,
man.
The Sheriff grew pale with alarm. The
Governor had left town on Saturday evening on a
hunting excursion to Richmond, and he knew
that there was therefore no possibility of obtaining
a formal respital of the men till a fresh execu-
tioner could be obtained. To postpone the execu-
tion on less authority than his Excellency’s he
dare not. It would invalidate the sentences.
Neither dare he contemplate the fearful but legally
imperative alternative of hanging the men him-
self. To hang a dozen men by proxy was one
thing; to adjust nooses and pull levers with his
own shapely hands was another. And then a
thought hashed into his mind which he told his
“ lady ” at dinner that evening he regarded as a
Providential inspiration. Acting upon it he ad-
dressed Bond.
“ Bond,” he said, “ I promise you life and a free
pardon if you will hang the rest.”
A terrible second, and Bond’s answer came clear
and decisive.
“No!”
The crowd was tremulous to its margin with de-
light. This was something not in the bill!
“Will you, MTCenzie? ”
“ No! ”
“ Argyle ? ”
“No!”
“ You, M ‘Gavin ? ”
“ I’ll see you in hell first! ” said M‘Gavin.
“ Smith ? ”
“ No ! ”
“ Travis, you ?”
“ Life on such terms ? No, sir . I ain’t a sneak !”
“Bennett, you? Remember, life and freedom !”
“Bah !”
“ Green ?”
“What would my people say if they knew such
a thing of me ?”
“Grafton think, man, what you refuse !”
Grafton thought for fully a minute. He gazed
at the sky. The fleck of cloud had vanished. Then
he said
“Will you throw the permanent billet of execu-
tioner in, sir ?”
There was a note of genuine relief in the Sheriff’s
voice as he replied : “Yes, certainly.” He thought
his troubles were, ended.
“ What is the screw, sir ?”
“ Thirty pounds a year, rations, and the usual
fee for each—ah —execution. ”
“Not good enough,” sneered the reprobate,
delighted at the way in which he had excited the
Sheriff’s hopes.
Away on the Cove the sentry on the quarter-
deck of H.M.S. Beagle was roused from semi-
slumber by the mighty roar of laughte” "diich
broke from the crowd at Grafton’s reply.
“Madden —you?”
“ Mr. Madden’s compliments to the Sherni, and
he respectfully declines the honour.”
Another roar from the multitude.
The tenth man had now refused life and freedom
on the Sheriff’s terms. Before Eyfe, the eleventh,
could be asked, Absalom Day, the only one who
had been “capped,” was observed to struggle
violently, as though he were already in the con-
vulsions of death. In his contortions he nearly
threw himself over the scaffold. As it would bo
an act of inhumanity to allow Day to suffocate,
even though it was designed to break his neck
in a few minutes’ time by due course of law, the
Sheriff ordered the cap to be taken off. Muggins
obeyed, and Absalom Day looked once more upon
the world.
Barely had his ghastly face been freed before he
gasped out : “ I’ll do it, Mr. Sheriff; I’ll hang ’em,
sir, if you’ll grant me life. I don’t want freedom,
sir—only life, sir, only life !”
“ It’s my chance first, Sheriff,” said Eyfe.
Day shrieked.
“ Will you do it, Eyfe ?” asked the Sheriff.
“ Oh, sir, I ’udn’t like to take th’ billet from
poor Day ;he hankers so arter it!” laughed the
wretch. “ Let him do it.”
• * *
Eleven dangling forms, a few minutes
afterwards, testified to the fact that Absalom Day
had “done it,” and had performed his share of the
bargain.
That the Sheriff performed his may be inferred
from the following extracts from the Hobart
Town Gazette of the week following the exe-
cution :
Promotion. —Absalom Day, native, from Javelin-man,
to executioner, vice Johnson, dismissed.
Dismissal.— Richard Johnson, No. 4563, per Rodney,
from his post of executioner for disobedience of orders.
To be sent to the chain-gang at the settlement to serve
the remainder of his sentence.
The details of floggings were not gazetted,
otherwise we would be also able to read in the files
of the official paper this
By order of the Comptroller-General, Richard Johnson,
No. 4563, per Rodney, 300 lashes, for gross disobedience
of orders. Scourger, Muggins.
# * *
The first of the Civil officials to leave the
scene was Chaplain Ford. In his progress towards
St. David’s, where he was to hold a christening
service, he was stopped by an acquaintance who
wished to introduce to him a young naturalist
who was voyaging on board the Beagle.
“Proud to know you, sir,” said Parson Ford,
effusively. “ I hope we shall be able to show you
a tiling or two in this colony before you leave.”
“ I don’t doubt it,” said the immortal Charles
Darwin —for hr was the young naturalist —
who had witnessed the execution; “I have
already got some new light on a subject on which
I am theorising—the kinship of man with the
lower orders of animal life. ”
“Indeed,” said the Chaplain ; “how very in-
teresting, to be sure !”
# * #
As (Icinoiifdrating the effective manner in
which the criminal classes were impressed by the
edifying ceremony of the morning, it may be men
tioned that, the same night, an atrocious murder
was perpetrated within half-a-mile of Hobart
Town gaol. The murderer was the ticket-of-
leave man who had volunteered “to ’sist.”
